Real Estate
Hawaii Mall Draws Hefty Price, Australian Investor; General Growth sells 25% stake in Honolulus Ala Moana Center
By Robbie Whelan
846 words
10 March 2015
17:33
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Sunny skies and hula dancers aren't the only things attracting foreigners to Hawaii. Add this to the list: one of the most valuable shopping centers in the country.

Mall giant General Growth Properties Inc. last week announced it had sold a 25% stake in Honolulu's Ala Moana Center, the world's largest open-air mall, for $1.37 billion.

The deal is notable for two reasons: its sky-high price and General Growth's partner, AustralianSuper, an $84 billion retirement fund making its first major foray into U.S. property investment.

Commercial real estate typically is valued based on its capitalization rate, or the annual net income produced by a property divided by the purchase price. Like bond yields, falling cap rates indicate rising values, and vice versa.

Last year, Ala Moana, a 2.2 million square-foot shopping center with 290 shops and 80 restaurants, produced $145 million in net operating income, according to Steve Sakwa, an analyst with Evercore ISI. Based on that, the mall traded at a cap rate of 2.9%, one of the lowest at which a mall has ever sold.

In comparison, the average mall cap rate in March stood at 5.2%, according to Green Street Advisors, a Newport Beach, Calif., property-research firm.

By the end of 2016, after a scheduled expansion is expected to add another 660,000 square feet of space, General Growth expects the mall to be producing about $215 million in net operating income. That would push the cap rate to roughly 3.9%, still extraordinarily low for the mall industry but perhaps not such a red flag.

If it's not the best mall asset in the country, it is definitely one of the top five," Mr. Sakwa said of Ala Moana. I'm not sure there's any other mall in the country that does over $200 million in net operating income a year."

General Growth, the country's second-largest mall owner by market value, has been pruning its portfolio since emerging from Chapter 11 bankruptcy nearly five years ago. In 2010, the company owned all or part of 169 malls. Today it has 121.

The real-estate investment trust also has started snapping up pricey urban retail properties, including properties on Fifth Avenue in Manhattan and on Michigan Avenue in Chicago, at top-dollar prices.

We've really been going through a major portfolio transformation," said Kevin Berry, General Growth's spokesman.

General Growth said it plans to use the proceeds from the sale of Ala Moana to pay down about $700 million in debt. Ala Moana also produces more than 10% of General Growth's annual net operating income, the company said, and selling a stake in it allows the company to reduce some of the risk associated with having such high exposure to one asset.

The buyer of the stake in the Hawaiian mall, AustralianSuper, is one of the largest Australian superannuation funds, which manage large pools of tax-advantaged retirement savings contributed by Australian workers and their employers. The funds are typically invested in a portfolio of equities, fixed-income securities and real assets.

Foreign buyers often find it easier to buy property assets in one-off deals from private sellers because it is easier to reach a deal quietly, with less competition than there is for REIT assets, said James Fetgatter, chief executive of the Association of Foreign Investors in Real Estate, a trade group.

A lot of the newer foreigners that have entered the market recently are playing it safe," Mr. Fetgatter said. They're buying core, stable, trophy assets with lower yields."

In recent years, as commercial property prices have risen, the market has seen several high-profile partnerships between foreign institutional investors and REITs. In November, for example, office landlord Boston Properties Inc. sold a 45% stake in three towers in New York and Boston to Norway's sovereign-wealth fund for $1.5 billion. General Growth has roughly 30 joint ventures with foreign institutional investors, though not all of them as large as the Ala Moana deal.

For the Australian fund, the Ala Moana represents a blue-chip real-estate investment, despite its high price tag and a recent decline in the value of the Australian dollar relative to U.S. currency.

The yields on U.S. property are better than fixed income and safer than equities," said Mark Delaney, AustralianSuper's deputy chief executive and chief investment officer. AustralianSuper is looking to increase its property allocation from about 8.3% to about 10% over the next three to four years, the fund said.

The REITs are by and large the main holder of high-quality assets, which are the type of assets we're targeting in the US," Mr. Delaney said. If you want to get high-quality assets, you have to deal with their owners."

Write to Robbie Whelan at robbie.whelan@wsj.com[mailto:robbie.whelan@wsj.com]

Global Finance: Greystar Buys U.K. Portfolio --- The $915 million deal is the latest in a string of transactions in U.K. student housing
By Art Patnaude
685 words
9 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

International investors are piling into U.K. student housing, with a flurry of deals showing renewed interest in this small corner of the property market.

Greystar Real Estate Partners of the U.S. has agreed to buy a student-housing portfolio for about GBP 600 million ($915 million) from U.K. private-equity group Round Hill Capital LLC, a person familiar with the deal said.

Last week, the Canada Pension Plan Investment Board spent GBP 1.1 billion to acquire the U.K.'s Liberty Living, while a company headed by Russian oligarch Mikhail Fridman paid GBP 532 million for another London student-housing portfolio.

Yields on office buildings and other traditional property types have fallen as investors flush with cash and able to borrow at low rates have pushed prices higher. That has some investors looking elsewhere for higher returns.

Traditionally, real-estate investing "has been focused around the main commercial property types of retail, office, and industrial," a February report from the Investment Property Forum, a U.K. industry body, said.

But investors' hunt for higher long-term returns is producing "significant changes in the market," said Jon Neale, head of U.K. research at broker JLL. The fastest-growing sectors are now those deemed "alternatives" -- like student-housing, hotels, health care and privately rented residential real estate -- GBP 11.6 billion of which traded in 2014, a 33% increase from 2013, JLL figures show.

It remains a small part of the GBP 65 billion of U.K. real estate traded last year, but alternatives "accounted for only a few percentage points of volumes just a few years ago," Mr. Neale said.

For U.K. student accommodation, the average investment yield has been 5.8% this year, compared with 5.1% on offices, according to Real Capital Analytics data.

The yield on student housing is down from 6.4% last year, a reflection of rising prices.

There were about $2.4 billion of student housing transactions in the U.K. in 2014, down 18% from 2013, according to Real Capital Analytics. U.S. firms Greystar, LaSalle Investment and Goldman Sachs have been among the biggest buyers in the last two years, the data show.

Last Friday, the Canadian Pension Plan bought 16,700 student rooms in over 40 residences across 17 cities -- as well as the company that manages the business -- from the Brandeaux Student Accommodation Fund.

This was the Canadian investor's largest-ever real-estate deal in Europe, and its first there in the alternative space, said Andrea Orlandi, head of European real-estate investments.

For CPP, student accommodation provides "a noticeable pickup in the yield" offered by office investments, Mr. Orlandi said.

The opportunity to buy the management company and its team means, "we can look to buy more assets" in a sector "that can, and will, consolidate in the future," he said.

The U.K. has long been a destination for foreign students looking to study at one of the renowned universities, or simply improve their English. London in particular is a magnet for foreign students.

Last Tuesday, Carlyle Group sold Pure Student Living -- with 2,170 student rooms across five central London sites -- to a Luxembourg-based investment vehicle called LetterOne Treasury Services, led by Mr. Fridman.

The deal saw Carlyle exit from U.K. student accommodation after a five-year run. But the U.S. investor "remains committed to student housing as a long-term institutional asset class," the firm said in a statement, noting it still has over 1,700 rooms in the Netherlands managed under The Student Hotel brand.

In the Greystar deal Monday, Round Hill is selling three London residences with 2,375 beds, a portfolio it bought for $415 million from Blackstone Group in 2012. Round Hill will retain the student-housing brand Nido, its management business and one student residence in London it bought earlier this year.

Chinese Real Estate Sees Deal Activity
By Esther Fung
494 words
3 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
22
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

SHANGHAI -- Companies dealing in real estate are combining in China as the property downturn lingers and the emergence of Internet services upends the business.

58.com Inc. -- the Chinese equivalent of Craigslist Inc., backed by Internet company Tencent Holdings Ltd. -- said Monday that it had acquired Shanghai-based property-listing platform Anjuke Inc. for about $267 million in cash and shares.

It marked the latest deal in China's fast-expanding market for services that connect online users with traditional offline businesses.

In another deal, announced on Sunday, Homelink Real Estate Agency Co., Beijing's largest real-estate firm by market share, will combine with Shanghai Deovolente Realty Co. Homelink, which is much larger than Deovolente in terms of employees and outlets, characterized the deal as a merger but didn't disclose terms.

Since 2011, real-estate companies have weathered a volatile market and the rise of China's e-commerce companies. Conditions have worsened since 2013 because of a huge supply of homes, especially in smaller cities. Housing sales in China fell 7.8% nationwide in 2014, and home prices in many smaller cities declined significantly, squeezing profits.

In February, average home prices in 100 Chinese cities fell 3.8% from a year earlier, the fifth straight month of year-over-year declines, according to private sector data provider China Real Estate Index System, an arm of SouFun Holdings Ltd.

Last year, more than a dozen property companies, including Deovolente and Homelink, announced a boycott of China's largest real estate Internet portal, SouFun, arguing that it charged unfair prices for listings on its website. SouFun disputes the accusations.

In January, Chinese real-estate firm Century 21 China Real Estate was delisted by the New York Stock Exchange after it failed to maintain the required market capitalization. The company has said the delisting won't deter it from increasing its business.

58.com said revenue growth from the housing sector slowed in the third quarter as a result of the housing slump. Some real-estate firms have cut back on the number of branches and on online marketing expenses, said Zhou Hao, 58.com's chief financial officer, according a transcript of a company conference call.

Anjuke, which was founded in 2007, sees 66 million unique visitors to its site each month, according to its website. Anjuke covers 67 cities in China and said it would continue to operate its website and mobile application under the Anjuke brand.

Deovolente, the Shanghai brokerage, said that after its combination with Homelink, the combined company would be called New Homelink. The combined firm will focus on developing its online-to-offline marketing strategies, Deovolente said.

Homelink will combine its 1,800 storefront outlets and 35,000 staff with Deovolente's more than 200 storefronts and 5,000 staff, it said.

---

Juro Osawa in Hong Kong contributed to this article.

Economy
Pending Home Sales Rose 1.7% in January; National Association of Realtors index points to firming demand
By Ben Leubsdorf
589 words
27 February 2015
11:33
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTONA forward-looking gauge of U.S. home purchases rose in January to its highest level in nearly a year and a half, a sign of firming demand in the housing market.

The National Association of Realtors said Friday that its pending home sales index[http://www.realtor.org/news-releases/2015/02/pending-home-sales-rise-in-january-to-highest-level-in-18-months], which is based on contract signings for purchases of existing homes, increased 1.7% to a seasonally adjusted level of 104.2 in January from an upwardly revised reading of 102.5 in December.

Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had expected pending home sales would rise 2% last month after dipping in December. Home sales typically close within a couple months after signing.

Through the volatility, the trend in home sales is up probably up modestly at least," High Frequency Economics chief U.S. economist Jim O'Sullivan said in a note to clients.

The index rose 8.4% in January from a year earlier and reached its highest level since August 2013, when home sales were tumbling after a jump in mortgage rates[http://blogs.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/economics/2014/05/19/higher-mortgage-rates-dragged-down-u-s-home-sales-san-francisco-fed/].

Lawrence Yun, NAR's chief economist, said sales look healthier despite tight inventory that is sending prices higher, a trend that could deter some would-be home buyers.

All indications point to modest sales gains as we head into the spring buying season," Mr. Yun said.

News Corp, owner of The Wall Street Journal, also owns Move Inc., which operates a website and mobile products for the National Association of Realtors.

The U.S. housing market entered 2015 on uncertain footing after a generally lackluster 2014.

Sales of previously owned homes, which make up about 90% of the U.S. market, fell roughly 3% in 2014 from the prior year. In January, existing-home sales declined 4.9%[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/u-s-existing-home-sales-down-4-9-in-january-1424703698] from the prior month to their slowest pace since last spring, the NAR said Monday.

Sales of newly built single-family homes ticked down 0.2% in January[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/u-s-new-home-sales-at-481-000-pace-in-january-1424876627] from the prior month but remained near their fastest pace since mid-2008, the Commerce Department said Wednesday. In 2014, new-home sales rose about 2% from the prior year.

Home-building activity dipped in January. Housing starts fell a seasonally adjusted 2%[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/u-s-housing-starts-slip-2-in-january-1424266345] from the prior month, and single-family housing starts declined 6.7% from December, according to Commerce Department data.

Still, starts rose 18.7% in January from a year earlier, reflecting a 16.3% increase in construction of single-family homes and even stronger growth for multifamily structures like apartment buildings.

Mortgage rates remain low but have ticked up in recent weeks. The average interest rate on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage was 3.80%[http://www.freddiemac.com/pmms/index.html?], Freddie Mac said Thursday, up from 3.67% last month but down from 4.30% in February 2014.

According to Friday's report, pending sales of existing homes rose 3.2% in the South last month from December, and climbed 2.2% in the West. Pending sales ticked up 0.1% in the Northeast and fell 0.7% in the Midwest.

The National Association of Realtors on Friday also released seasonal-adjustment revisions for pending home sales data from 2012 through 2014, and revised data for the West region going back to 2001. Most revisions are minor with no impact on previous characterizations of the overall market based on the headline seasonally adjusted index," the trade group said in a statement.

U.S. News: U.S. Watch
488 words
26 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A5
English
Copyright 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

HOUSING

New-Home Sales Falter

At Beginning of Year

New-home sales fell slightly in January but the overall pace remained near a six-year high, a sign of strong demand that could help boost the U.S. housing market.

Sales of newly built, single-family homes decreased 0.2% from a month earlier to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 481,000, the Commerce Department said Wednesday. Figures for the prior month were revised up to a rate of 482,000 from an initial estimate of 481,000, making December the strongest month since June 2008.

Wednesday's data showed inventories of new homes held fairly steady. At January's pace, it would take 5.4 months to exhaust the supply. The median price of a newly built home stood at $294,300 in January, up 9.1% from $269,800 a year earlier.

Separately, the Mortgage Bankers Association said in a report Wednesday that the rate of borrowers falling behind on their home loans or in foreclosure dropped to the lowest levels since 2007.

In the last three months of 2014, 5.7% of borrowers were at least one payment behind on their mortgage, but not in foreclosure, the MBA said. The percentage of loans in some stage of the foreclosure process in the fourth quarter was 2.3%.

-- WSJ Staff

---

WISCONSIN

State Senate Passes

Union-Limiting Bill

The Wisconsin Senate passed legislation late Wednesday to limit union powers amid a second day of protests here as the state capitol again becomes a battleground over the future of organized labor.

The GOP-controlled Senate passed a "right-to-work" bill with a 17-15 vote that would allow employees in unionized private-sector workplaces to opt out of paying union dues.

Republicans also control the state Assembly, making passage likely in the coming days. Gov. Scott Walker -- who is considering a 2016 run for the Republican presidential nomination-- has said he would sign such a measure into law.

Immediately after passage the spectator gallery erupted in boos and chants of "shame, shame!"

The bill comes four years after Mr. Walker pushed through legislation limiting the reach of public-sector unions, drawing tens of thousands to protest in the capitol and launching a contentious recall election, which the governor won.

Sen. Scott Fitzgerald, the majority leader, said the bill would create a more competitive state economy and give workers more individual freedom to choose union membership. He added that the measure doesn't ban collective bargaining between unions and employers.

The legislation still faces opposition from unions and Democratic lawmakers, who argue it is meant to undermine organized labor and won't deliver the economic benefits backers promise. "It's bad for the working men and women of this state, both union and non-union," said Sen. Dave Hansen, a Democrat.

-- Ben Kesling

Heard on the Street
America's Fixer-Upper Housing Market
By Justin Lahart
270 words
26 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]

It isn't a great time to be in the business of selling homes. But fixing them up? That is a different story.

The Commerce Department on Wednesday said that builders in January sold 481,000 new, single-family homes at a seasonally adjusted annual rate. That was near the highest level since 2008, but still well below the levels of the 1990s and early 2000s. This came on top of disappointing January existing-home sales figures from the National Association of Realtors on Monday.

In contrast, the home-improvement industry is doing well: Witness strong results this week from Lowe's Cos. and Home Depot Inc. This reflects a broader trend. Commerce Department data show that in real terms, people are spending as much on home and garden tools and supplies as they were in 2007.

Home improvement's strength amid continued weakness in home sales represents a break in the trend that existed up until a few years ago, when they were more closely related. A simple linear regression analysis shows that if the same relationship seen in the period between 2002 and 2010 held, the real home improvement sales figure in December would have been associated with combined sales of 7 million new and existing homes, at an annual rate. That is about 1.4 million higher than what was actually recorded. Rather than buy their dream home, people are trying to make the home they already own look more like it.

Cushman & Wakefield Is Put on the Block
By Peter Grant
935 words
26 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
22
English
(Copyright (c) 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The Italian family that controls Cushman & Wakefield Inc., one of the world's largest real-estate services firms, is putting the company up for sale as rising property prices push up the value of rivals, according to people familiar with the matter.

Exor SpA, the investment arm of the Agnelli family, has approved management's hiring of Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Morgan Stanley to help look for a buyer for Cushman, the people said. Exor prefers a privately held buyer and wouldn't consider selling to a direct competitor, the people said.

Commercial real-estate analysts say Cushman & Wakefield could fetch as much as $2 billion. The family paid $565.4 million for a 67.5% stake in Cushman eight years ago and has since boosted that to 81%.

When real-estate markets heat up, values can rise for a broad swath of companies that don't own property but profit from transactions and other services. Commercial real-estate services firms have branched out from their traditional roles as brokers to provide a wide range of consulting, appraisal, asset and property management and other functions.

Cushman is the third-largest property-services firm in the world, after Los Angeles-based CBRE Group Inc., and JLL of Chicago, analysts say. Cushman recorded $163 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, or Ebitda, in 12 months that ended September 2014.

A sharp increase in mergers and acquisitions among real-estate services businesses over the past few years has helped send prices for such firms to more than 10 times Ebitda.

In a written statement Tuesday, a spokeswoman for Cushman said the firm and Exor "continually seek ways to further enhance the businesses, create value and further accelerate" their plans. She added: "There is currently no transaction to disclose, nor guarantee that such a review may result in any transaction."

A sale at such a multiple would be a vindication for the Agnelli family, best known for their control of Fiat Chrysler Automobiles NV. The family took control of Cushman in late 2006, just before the market crashed. During the early years of the downturn, Cushman suffered losses and management upheaval as the pace of real-estate sales and leases slowed to a crawl.

But commercial real estate has rebounded strongly in the current low-interest-rate environment. Investors are paying record prices for office buildings, stores, hotels and other properties, believing their returns will beat bonds and be less volatile than stocks.

The sales surge in turn has boosted brokerage commissions for real-estate services firms, which has attracted investors to the sector. Shares of publicly listed CBRE on Tuesday were near their 2007 peak, while JLL was just below its record high.

Meanwhile, big investment firms have been looking to acquire services firms. Last year, a venture led by private-equities giant TPG paid $1.05 billion for Chicago-based DTZ and merged it with Cassidy Turley, a Washington firm valued at up to $600 million.

BGC Partners Inc., a Cantor Fitzgerald spinoff, has been investing heavily to grow services firm Newmark Grubb Knight Frank. Earlier this month, Toronto-based FirstService Corp. announced plans to spin off its commercial-real-estate unit, Colliers International, as a separate publicly traded company.

Cushman, which will celebrate its 100th birthday in two years, has benefited from the market's recovery and more stable management in the past year following a period of tension with Exor executives.

The Agnellis' experience with Cushman dates to the mid-1980s, when the family that co-founded Fiat acquired a minority stake in Rockefeller Group Inc., which owned Cushman. Rockefeller Group later was purchased by Mitsubishi Estate Co., but in 2006 the Agnellis acquired a 67.5% stake in Cushman from Mitsubishi. Firm employees hold the 19% of Cushman not owned by the Agnellis

The crash of 2008 hit the company hard. Cushman posted a $127 million loss in 2009 and was hurt by the departure of a number of top-performing brokers. In 2010, it brought in a turnaround expert Glenn Rufrano as chief executive.

Mr. Rufrano improved the firm's performance but clashed with Exor's chief operating officer, Shahriar Tadjbakhsh, a former Goldman Sachs executive who played a lead role in overseeing the real-estate firm. Mr. Rufrano abruptly left in 2013 before his contract ended following a dispute over his desire to reinvest Cushman's profits rather than pay them to Exor.

A flurry of management changes followed. Former Goldman Sachsexecutive Edward Forst took over as chief executive. Mr. Tadjbakhsh left Exor and John Elkann, Exor's chairman and the great, great grandson of Fiat's co-founder, became chairman of Cushman.

Messrs. Forst and Elkann agreed on strategy. In December, the Exor board approved Mr. Forst's three-year strategic plan to spend more than $500 million on technology and acquisitions of firms and talent in top cities, according to people familiar with the matter.

Cushman quickly moved ahead on that strategy by acquiring Massey Knakal Realty Services, a leading New York brokerage, for $100 million.

The effort to find a new owner for Cushman will be overseen by Mr. Forst, who is supportive of the sale because he is hopeful a buyer would fuel the firm's expansion more rapidly, according to people familiar with the matter. At this point he and Exor executives aren't interested in becoming a public company because they believe Cushman can be more competitive in private hands.

Real Estate
Cushman & Wakefield Going Up for Sale; Rising Property Prices Pushing Up Value of Rivals
By Peter Grant
986 words
24 February 2015
19:27
The Wall Street Journal Online

English

At this point he and Exor executives aren't interested in becoming a public company because they believe Cushman can be more competitive in private hands.

They aren't willing to talk to competitors at this point because they believe much of the firm's value is in its brand and 98-year-old culture, said people familiar with the matter.

The Property Report
Norway Fund Grabbing Real Estate --- Giant pension fund is hoping to improve its investment returns by taking on more risk
By Alessia Pirolo and Kjetil Malkenes Hovland
917 words
25 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Norway's state-run investment fund is becoming one of the world's biggest landlords at a time when other big commercial-property owners are sounding alarms about high prices.

The $874 billion Government Pension Fund of Norway, the world's largest sovereign-wealth fund, last year purchased $7.6 billion of property globally, more than any other sovereign fund, according to data from real-estate tracker Real Capital Analytics.

The push is part of a broader strategy to improve returns by taking on more risk. The fund, which has generated an annual return of 3.7% since 1998, aims over time to boost its real-estate holdings to 5% from the current 1.3%.

Earlier this month, the fund closed on its purchase of a 45% stake in a 40-story skyscraper at 11 Times Square in Manhattan for $401.9 million, a deal that valued the 1.1 million square foot property at $1,273 a square foot, which is at the high end of the range of prices being paid for Midtown office buildings in recent years.

In January, meanwhile, the fund beat six other bidders in London for Queensberry House, a 75,000-square-foot retail and office building, agreeing to pay GBP 190.6 million ($294.6 million.) The seller, Sorgente Group SpA, had paid GBP 167 million for the property in 2012.

But some experts question the timing of the Norway fund's strategy of bulking up on office buildings, stores, warehouses and other commercial property in Europe and the U.S. Prices have risen so rapidly in the past two years that seasoned investors have begun to move to the sidelines even as Norway piles in.

"Asset prices today are high, and acquisitions are getting dicey," said Steven Roth, chief executive of Vornado Realty Trust, a leading U.S. real-estate investment trust, on an investor call earlier this month. "We are now at the point in the cycle where the easy money has been made, and it seems to me that this is a better time to harvest than to invest."

As a sign of how high values have risen in London, office-building investors in December paid prices that will give them an initial yield of just 4% from the incomes of the properties, according to data from Real Capital Analytics.

That was the lowest level since 2008.

The Norway fund is the main vehicle through which the Norwegian people benefit from the country's position as the world's third-largest gas exporter and the 10th-largest oil exporter. Its assets have more than doubled since 2010, and its current value is equal to about $170,000 per Norwegian citizen.

The fund, which launched in 1990 and received its first cash transfers in 1996, initially invested only in stocks and bonds. But average annual returns have fallen short of the government's long-term return target of 4%. Hoping to boost returns, the government in 2010 allowed the fund to invest up to 5% of its value in real estate.

Norwegian central-bank governor Oystein Olsen in a speech earlier this month said that with long-term government bond yields near zero, Norway's fund return could drop "perhaps below 3%."

The fund also has been hurt by falling global energy prices, which have cut cash transfers from the Norwegian government.

A spokeswoman for Norges Bank Investment Management, which oversees the fund, said it is trying to achieve the "highest possible return with a moderate risk level."

Asked about the timing of the fund's increased appetite for real estate, she said: "We are a long-term investor" and that the fund's strategy is to focus on large global cities like New York and San Francisco, where "the prospects for solid long-term return are good."

The fund has been especially active in the U.S. In 2014, Norway was the second-largest foreign investor in U.S. real estate, after Canada, thanks mainly to acquisitions by its sovereign-wealth fund, according to a report by CBRE Group Inc. Norwegian real-estate investments in the U.S. totaled $4.4 billion in 2014, up 120% from 2013, the report said.

Yet only 1.3% of the fund's portfolio was in real estate as of the third quarter of 2014. That means it will take much more buying to meet the 5% target -- at a time when prices have risen sharply from their recession lows.

In the U.S., values are at record levels, 17% above their precrash high in 2007, according to Green Street.

Fund executives in June said they planned to invest an additional 1% of its value each year in real estate through 2016. Last year, the fund also hired a separate executive group for real estate and plans to add 200 people to its staff of around 400 by 2016.

Some real-estate investors question the fund's strategy of paying top-dollar for properties. While annual income for well-leased top-quality properties typically amounts to between 4% and 4.5% of a property's sale price, Norway has made multiple purchases in which the income is below 4%, a low number in the real-estate sector.

---

Eliot Brown contributed to this article.

Business Watch: News Digest
573 words
24 February 2015
21:52
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Small Contractors Boost Bottom Line

Home Depot Inc. said strong demand from small professional contractors for everything from compressors to roofing helped boost sales in the latest quarter, a sign of strength in the housing market.

Small contractors play a large role in Home Depot's business, accounting for 3% of customers and 35% of its sales, according to Chief Financial Officer Carol Tom. Their health is a proxy for the housing market," she said. As housing recovers they have work and are spending more in our stores."

Suzanne Kapner

Sycamore Partners Abandons Chico's Bid

Sycamore Partners has abandoned its attempt to buy women's clothing retailer Chico's FAS Inc. after failing to line up financing for the deal on acceptable terms, according to a person familiar with the matter.

The Wall Street Journal reported two weeks ago that the New York buyout firm was in advanced talks to take Chico's private.

The Fort Myers, Fla., company had a market capitalization of $2.8 billion based on its closing share price Tuesday of $18.11. Using typical takeover prices, a deal could have valued Chico's at more than $3 billion, making it one the largest private-equity buyouts this year.

But the potential deal recently fell apart for a reason all too familiar to Sycamore, which in January ended buyout talks with Express Inc., another clothing retailer that focuses on young adults. Private-equity firms typically fund their deals using some of their own cash but mainly with debt.

Bankers and others say it has become increasingly hard to finance deals for mall-based retailers, which are beset by declining traffic, lackluster sales and increased competition from e-commerce.

In addition to its namesake stores, which tend to cater to older women, Chico's has other brands, including White House Black Market, lingerie label Soma, and online and catalog retailer Boston Proper. Across its brands, Chico's has over 1,550 stores throughout the U.S. and Canada, according to its website.

Dana Mattioi

Members Join Board in a Compromise

Juniper Networks Inc. has agreed to add two new independent directors to its board in a compromise with activist investor Elliott Management Corp. over board representation.

The Wall Street Journal reported in December that Elliott, which owns a roughly 9% stake in Juniper, had privately recommended to the Sunnyvale, Calif., company a number of new director candidates in an effort to add more industry experience to its 11-person board and help improve Juniper's operations after the company suffered setbacks, according to people familiar with the matter.

Juniper said Tuesday that it has appointed Jim Dolce, chief executive of mobile security company Lookout, and Rahul Merchant, who runs his own advisory firm, to its board.

The appointments are effective March 1. Juniper said it identified the candidates through a search process started last year that also evaluated Elliott's recommendations.

Elliott, meanwhile, has agreed to support Juniper's board nominees at its annual meeting.

Juniper, one the largest competitors to industry giant Cisco Systems Inc., makes routing and switching systems and other equipment used by telecommunications companies and other big network operators.

Juniper has struggled with lower-than-expected demand, particularly in the U.S.

Elliott first disclosed its stake in Juniper more than a year ago. Elliott has since convinced the company to cut costs and buy back more stock.

Chelsey Dulaney

Economy
Growth in U.S. Home Prices Slowed in 2014; S&P/Case-Shiller national index posts weakest full-year gain since home prices were falling in 2011
By Kathleen Madigan
470 words
24 February 2015
09:21
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Home prices in 2014 saw yearly growth slow to the weakest pace in three years, according to a home price report released Tuesday.

The home price index covering the entire nation rose 4.6% in the 12 months ended in December, said the S&P/Case-Shiller Home Price Index report. That is down slightly from 4.7% in November and is the weakest full-year gain since home prices were falling in 2011.

Narrower measures of home prices also showed a small acceleration in December but full-year growth still was the lowest since 2011.

The home price index covering 10 major U.S. cities increased 4.3% in the year ended in December from a 4.2% rise in November. The 20-city price index was up 4.5%. That is above the 4.3% advance posted in November and equal to the 4.5% expected by economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal.

The report said average home prices in the 10 and 20 cities covered are back to levels last seen in autumn 2004, but are still down between 16% and 17% from their peaks set in mid-2006.

On an unadjusted basis, the 10-city and 20-city gauges increased 0.1% in December over November, while the national index fell 0.1%.

Seasonally adjusted, the 10-city index increased 0.8%, the 20-city gauge was up 0.9% and the U.S. index advanced 0.7% in December.

The S&P/Case-Shiller report covers sale prices of existing homes, which have been weak lately. The National Association of Realtors said Monday that January resales fell 4.9%[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/u-s-existing-home-sales-down-4-9-in-january-1424703698], but some of the weakness reflected low inventories of homes for sale. Tight supplies would tend to support higher prices.

The housing recovery is faltering. While prices and sales of existing homes are close to normal, construction and new home sales remain weak," said David M. Blitzer, chairman of the Index Committee at S&PDow Jones Indices. The softness in housing is despite favorable conditions elsewhere in the economy: strong job growth, a declining unemployment rate, continued low interest rates and positive consumer confidence."

Regionally, San Francisco and Miami saw the strongest 12-month increases while Chicago saw an average home price increase of just 1.3%.

Corrections & Amplifications

The home price index covering the entire nation rose 4.6% in the 12 months ended in December, down slightly from 4.7% in November. An earlier version of this story said the index was up slightly from November.

U.S. News: Existing Home Sales Drop at Start of Year
By Jeffrey Sparshott
466 words
24 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

WASHINGTON -- Sales of previously owned homes slowed in January, a reflection of the rising prices and tight supplies that could constrain the housing market this year.

Existing-home sales fell 4.9% last month from December to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.82 million, the National Association of Realtors said on Monday, the slowest pace in nine months.

Weather didn't appear to be a big factor in the report, which showed sales falling across all regions of the country. While New England was buffeted by blizzard conditions, January overall was warm and dry in the contiguous U.S., according to the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration.

The median sale price for a previously owned home, meanwhile, rose 6.2% from a year earlier to $199,600. Year over year, prices have climbed for 35 straight months.

"One of the big problems is the supply of homes," Joel Naroff, chief economist at Naroff Economic Advisors, said in a note to clients. "Basically, buyers have limited options as the inventory is hovering near some of the lowest levels we have seen in the last fifteen years."

At the current sales pace, it would take about 4.7 months to exhaust the supply of homes on the market, the Realtors association said.

Construction of more homes could help ease the situation, but building also has remained subdued.

U.S. housing starts fell 2% from a month earlier to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.065 million in January, the Commerce Department said last week.

Starts on single-family units, which exclude apartments and represent almost two-thirds of the market, dropped 6.7% in January from December.

"I would say that the initial phase of price recovery was good news for the economy but now that prices are easily outpacing people's income and general consumer price inflation, I would say it's less healthy," said Lawrence Yun, the NAR's chief economist. "We need to moderate the price growth, and that can only happen with an increase in inventory."

Housing has been a missing piece of the recovery, making only a small contribution to overall economic growth last year. Many economists expect sales to accelerate this year amid steady hiring, still-low interest rates and the formation of new households, though the year is off to a bumpy start.

Last year, existing-home sales, which account for about 90% of all purchases in the U.S., fell slightly from 2013 and remain well shy of prerecession levels.

News Corp, owner of The Wall Street Journal, also owns Move Inc., which operates a website and mobile products for the National Association of Realtors.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By John Carney
412 words
23 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Housewarming May Be in Store

Despite the arctic air blasting the central and eastern U.S., the housing market is likely to show signs of a chill rather than a deep freeze.

January was warmer than average for the nation as a whole, according to the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Association. Don't tell Boston residents, but seven western states, including California, had one of the 10 warmest Januarys on record.

That could moderate the decline in sales of existing homes expected Monday when the National Association of Realtors releases data for January.

Economists estimate sales falling from a month earlier but up from the prior year, when severe weather caused the housing market to contract sharply. Zillow forecasts a report of a 2.2% decline in January from December to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.93 million units.

One reason for the expected decline is that pending home sales, which lead actual purchases by a month or two, fell in December, according to the National Association of Realtors. Also, housing starts dropped in January, according to the Commerce Department. Rising home prices as well as the decline in housing inventory in the last few months of 2014 also likely slowed purchases.

While the strengthening jobs market is a plus, the overall economy might not be as favorable for sales. The Federal Reserve Bank of Atlanta last week lowered its forecast for economic growth in the first quarter to a seasonally adjusted annualized rate of 1.9% from 2.2%. And while rates fell steeply in January, with the 30-year fixed-rate mortgage averaging 3.67% for the month, this likely won't provide an immediate boost to housing.

All isn't lost, though: Lower rates likely encouraged home shopping that may lead to higher sales in the spring. And expectations that the Federal Reserve will raise interest rates later this year could also contribute to a spring thaw, as buyers look to lock in loans before that.

Another tailwind is likely to be easier lending conditions. The Mortgage Bankers Association's credit-availability index showed improvement in January. This appears to be the result of banks responding to changes in the rules around mortgages purchased by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac.

So even if data due Monday reflect a colder market, there is reason to expect a warmer spring.

MANSION --- Spread Sheet: A Pot of Gold -- or a Money Pit? --- Fixing up a home is worth more in some places than others; Realtor.com identifies the U.S. cities whose homes for sale offer the biggest price break for buyers willing to put in a little work -- as well as those where the payoff is minimal; Some repairs are worth more than others: Beware a bad roof
By Adam Bonislawski
537 words
20 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
M7
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Feeling handy? Take a look at Clarksville, Tenn.

According to numbers from Realtor.com, the city offers the country's biggest price break for home buyers willing to put in a little work. The median price for a fixer-upper in Clarksville is $69,900, an almost 70% discount off the $226,000 median price for a home in good condition. (The analysis focused on houses in the 2,000-square-foot to 3,000-square-foot range.)

The only challenge is finding one. Of the 806 Clarksville homes on the market in December 2014, just three were handyman specials.

Fixer-uppers are an increasingly popular option for home buyers looking to save a little money in exchange for a bit of elbow grease, says Leslie Piper, a real-estate agent with San Francisco Bay Area real-estate firm Pacific Union.

But where are the best spots for buying such properties? Based on the percentage, as of December 2014, of home listings that included repair-related terms like "fixer," "as-is" and "TLC," the Realtor.com analysis identified both the markets with the most fixer-uppers available and the places where buying one offers the biggest potential price break.

In addition to Clarksville, towns like Omaha, Neb. (53%), Albany, N.Y. (50%), and Greensboro, N.C. (49%), offer significant savings, but, again, supplies are limited -- these towns' stocks of fixer-uppers number 10, 23 and 1, respectively.

On the other hand, buyers will likely find happier hunting, but smaller discounts, in spots like Atlanta (288 listings, 37% discount), Tampa, Fla. (223 listings, 32% discount) and Philadelphia (348 listings, 17% discount).

And then there are locales where buying a house in need of TLC will scarcely score any savings at all. In Portland, Ore., the median fixer-upper runs $354,928, just 6% lower than the $379,000 median price of a home in good condition. In Prescott, Ariz., the discount is a mere 2%. Lorinda Johnson, an associate broker at Prescott Realty, attributes this small spread to what she says is the town's stable, retiree-heavy homeowner base.

News Corp., parent of Wall Street Journal owner Dow Jones, owns Move Inc., parent of Realtor.com.

Regardless of where they settle, buyers considering a fixer-upper should make sure to get estimates from qualified tradespeople to determine just how much they'll have to sink into the property to bring it up to snuff, says Ms. Piper.

Some repairs, she notes, might be more trouble than expected. Issues like a bad roof or dry rot, for instance, "can be more costly than a buyer might recognize" she says, while problems like open subfloors and exposed wiring represent safety hazards that could make it difficult to get a mortgage.

License this article from Dow Jones Reprint Service[http://www.djreprints.com/link/DJRFactiva.html?FACTIVA=wjco20150220000010]

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Economy
U.S. Housing Starts Slip 2%; Permits Down, Too; Economists still see slowly recovering market
By Josh Mitchell
663 words
18 February 2015
10:49
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTONBuilders slowed new-home construction last month after a brisk December, pointing to uneven demand in an otherwise slowly recovering housing market.

U.S. housing starts fell 2% in January from a month earlier to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.065 million, the Commerce Department said Wednesday. That reflected a big drop in groundbreakings on single-family units, which exclude apartments and reflect the bulk of the market.

New applications for building permits, a bellwether for construction in coming months, slipped 0.7%.

Home construction is choppy, but starts were up almost 19% from a year earlier in January, reflecting gradual progress. Economists believe the market is primed for a pickup as the labor market improves, though significant obstacles remain, including tight credit conditions and lack of savings by consumers.

Despite the drop in starts and permits in January, the gradual housing market recovery of the past few years continues," PNC Financial Services Group senior economist Gus Faucher said in a note to clients. The fundamentals for housing demand are improving: job growth has accelerated over the past year, consumer confidence is rising, interest rates remain very low on an historical basis, and it is becoming easier to obtain credit. There is also significant pent-up demand for new homes after a dearth of building in the wake of the Great Recession."

Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had expected January housing starts and permits to both reach a rate of 1.07 million.

Home-construction figures are volatile and often revised. Wednesday's report showed new-home construction rose 7.1% in December from a month earlier, up from the initially reported 4.4%. But November's figure was revised down sharply, with starts falling 7.1%.

January's figure may have been affected by adverse weather.

The broader trend shows a gradually healing market from the crash. Housing starts were up 18.7% in January from a year earlier while permits were up 8.1%.

Conditions are ripe for a stronger housing market. Interest rates are hovering near historic lows, to an average 3.69% for a 30-year fixed mortgage last week[http://www.marketwatch.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/story/mortgage-rates-rise-as-30-year-climbs-to-369-freddie-mac-2015-02-12], according to Freddie Mac. Job growth has picked up, with employers expanding payrolls by an average 336,000 over the past three months.

But a run-up in home prices and tight lending standards are preventing many families from obtaining mortgages. Those who are buying are generally opting for cheaper previously owned homes rather than new ones.

"Demand for housing continues to be positive but restrained against the backdrop of minimal wage growth and lackluster savings," Sterne Agee economist Lindsey Piegza said in a note to clients Tuesday. While home prices have slowed their rate of annual appreciation, prices are still far outpacing wage growth, making it difficult for many new home buyers to move into the market."

Housing starts peaked in early 2006, surpassing a rate of 2.27 million during the height of the housing boom. After the crashed, they fell to a rate of below 500,000 in 2009. A long-awaited pickup in home-building would boost the economy by creating construction jobs and generating household spending on items like furniture.

Overall housing demand has been flagging. Sales of previously owned homes-reflecting about 90% of the market-picked up more than 2% in December from a month earlier. But sales in all of 2014 were down from 2013.

Home builders appear to be slightly less confident in the market than earlier this fall. A measure of home-builder confidence slipped to 55 this month from 57 in January[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/home-builder-confidence-dips-in-february-1424185202?KEYWORDS=home builder], the industry's National Association of Home Builders said Tuesday. Still, the above-50 reading indicates most builders remain optimistic about market conditions.

U.S. News: Builders Curb Their Enthusiasm a Little
By Kate Davidson
465 words
18 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

A measure of home-builder confidence dipped in February, a sign that the housing sector may continue to face headwinds.

The National Association of Home Builders/Wells Fargo Housing Market Index of builder confidence in the market for new single-family homes, released Tuesday, fell by two points to a seasonally adjusted level of 55 in February from January's reading of 57. A reading over 50 means a majority of builders see conditions as generally positive.

Home-builder sentiment trended up last year before reaching a peak of 59 in September, its highest level since 2005. But that upward movement has faltered over the past few months.

Still, the sentiment index has remained above 50 for eight consecutive months. The trade group attributed this month's downturn to unusually high snow levels in parts of the country.

Several factors in the economy would appear to support stronger home sales in 2015. Among them:

Employers added the most jobs in 2014 than any year since 1999 and continued to hire steadily in January, the Labor Department said. Lower unemployment typically brings more buyers into the housing market.

Interest rates on mortgage loans are near historic lows. The average interest rate on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage was 3.69% last week, down from an average of 4.3% in February 2014, according to Freddie Mac.

"Solid job growth, affordable home prices and historically low mortgage rates should help unleash growing pent-up demand and keep the housing market moving forward in the year ahead," said David Crowe, the trade group's chief economist.

Regionally, the three-month moving averages for the index improved this month in the West but slipped in the Northeast, Midwest and South.

The NAHB index is seasonally adjusted, but those adjustments can't always account for shifting weather patterns.

"We know from last year's experience during the polar vortex, when the headline index fell 10 points, that the NAHB survey is extremely susceptible to severe weather, so we can't right now view it as a reliable indicator of the underlying trend in housing market activity," Ian Shepherdson, chief economist for Pantheon Macroeconomics, said in a note.

Indeed, the NAHB index has traditionally tracked closely with single-family housing starts. But in recent years, the sentiment gauge has climbed much faster than the actual construction of homes has increased.

Now, there is hope that the market is revving up after years of malaise. Builders broke ground on single-family homes at the fastest rate in more than six years, with U.S. housing starts up 4.4% in December from a month earlier, the Commerce Department said last month.

Amid Ups, Housing Downers in Parts of New York City; Median Sales Prices Fell in 89 Neighborhoods Last Year From 2013
By Anne Kadet
948 words
13 February 2015
16:03
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

They're impossible to escape. The awful, record-setting real-estate numbers jump out of the newspaper, creep into one's Facebook feed and infiltrate the diner talk.

According to various reports, the average sale price in Manhattan rose 19% last year. Condo prices in brownstone Brooklyn climbed 37%. And for the first time, a property has shattered the $100 million mark.

If you're not already a homeowner, you might be thinking your future shelter will consist of a cardboard box.

And yet, the median sales price citywide for all properties climbed a modest 2.2% in 2014. That means there must be some neighborhoods where prices aredare one imagine?falling.

I asked StreetEasy, a local subsidiary of real-estate database Zillow, to crunch the numbers. And the results were soothing. Of the 287 neighborhoods showing property sales, 89 saw declines in their median prices.

Even better, a ranking of the bottom 10 includes some intriguing, charming neighborhoods.

Take St. George, at the northern tip of Staten Island. Median prices in this eccentric, steeply hilled jumble of Victorians and '60s townhouses were down 30.7% last year, to $237,810. With all the new development coming to the North Shore, this sounds like an opportunity.

Then there is Prospect Lefferts Gardens in Brooklyn, a historic swath of mansions and townhouses just east of Prospect Park. With the median down 19.9% last year, to $425,880, it remains one of the last truly lovely Brooklyn neighborhoods where prices haven't reached soul-crushing levels.

Some neighborhoods on the list were new to me, including Pelham Parkway, a central Bronx community where prices fell 21.6% last year, to $258,675. I took the 5 train uptown for a peek.

You've never seen so much brick in your life. Stretching west from Bronx Parkwhich includes the Bronx Zoo and New York Botanical Gardensolid six-story Tudor and Art Deco apartment buildings give way to graceful, single-family homes. The quiet, sloping streets meet at pleasing angles.

It's a racially mixed, working-class neighborhoodhalf the cars lining the blocks seem to be Toyota Corollas. The neighborhood was plastered with fliers for the annual Albanian Valentine dinner. You can shop at the Last Chance Market grocery or the Star Furniture store, which also offers iPhone screen replacement and MoneyGrams.

The best part: an abundance of front-patio dog statuary.

The biggest surprise on the list, though, was Midtown Souththe swath below 40th Street. People actually live there?

Paying special attention to the blocks between Madison and Fifth avenues, where the area's few apartment buildings are concentrated, I considered the neighborhood through a resident's eyes.

One could buy groceries at the halal food cart, load up on wholesale costume jewelry, bump into neighbors at Penn Station or explore a dozen souvenir shops.

But there are also a ton of dry cleaners, bakeries and hardware stores tucked into the side streets.

So why did these fine neighborhoods see price drops? I reached out to local real-estate brokers to get the scoop. That's when the trouble started.

Most ignored my calls and emails entirely, the way you might shun an annoying child asking too many questions about sex and death.

Others questioned my entire premise, admonishing me to get my facts straight.

Bronx broker Musa Balidemaj was typical. The information you got doesn't make sense. It's ridiculous!" he said when asked about Pelham Parkway.

He said the area had been defined too broadly. He prefers to focus on a small, genteel subsection where prices rose 15%.

New York, it seems, is a city where all the neighborhoods are above average.

Welcome to my world," says Gregory Heym, an economist with brokerage Brown Harris Stevens.

He often hears from brokers who don't understand why the numbers in his semiannual reports don't jibe with their experience, especially when he reports that prices are falling.

The problem: The numbers reflect sales prices, not property values, and there is a difference. When I issue a report and say the average price is up 10%, people assume their apartment is worth 10% more than a year ago, but that's not true," says Mr. Heym.

In a neighborhood with a lot of expensive new development, for example, the median price will climb even if older properties in the neighborhood haven't appreciated.

Median prices are also affected by the types of properties selling, says StreetEasy data scientist Alan Lightfeldt. If a lot of townhouses sell one year and cheaper one-bedrooms the next, the reported median price will fall even if values are climbing.

Of course, as anyone following the news can attest, brokers only argue with the numbers when they go down.

An exception: Drew Glick, a Brown Harris Stevens broker who has sold a few properties in Midtown South. While the area's low sales volume makes it tricky to spot trends, he says, it is true that the area isn't Manhattan's most desirable community.

While it attracts international buyers seeking a convenient pied--terre, it is seldom considered by locals looking to buy. And for that reason, it offers good deals. You can get more for your money," he says.

Here's the rub: While there is a 19.4% discount on Midtown South compared with the prior year, the median price is still a hefty $922,500.

We'll have to wait for the Groupon deal.

MANSION --- U.K. Closes a Tax Gap --- Homeowners from overseas face new levy when selling; brokers fear pain of capital gains
By Ruth Bloomfield
896 words
13 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
M1
English
Copyright 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Corrections & Amplifications

Mark Clegg is the head of residential investment at Cluttons estate agents in London. A Mansion article on Friday about the impact of capital-gains taxes on overseas homeowners incorrectly identified him as Martin Clegg.

(WSJ Feb. 14, 2015)

(END)

For the overseas investors who have funneled billions of dollars into London real estate over the past few years, the party may not be over, but it's about to become more subdued.

The British government is acting to close a long-standing loophole in its tax code: In April, for the first time, U.K. homeowners from overseas will need to pay capital-gains tax when they sell their property.

The move means the taxman will collect up to 28% of the profit earned on a property at the point of sale -- the same tax that British home sellers pay.

The charge is not retroactive and will only apply to the price differential between an official valuation for the property for April and its eventual sale price.

The new capital-gains tax comes on the heels of an overall increase in stamp duty introduced last year that has increased buying costs for properties priced at GBP 1 million (about $1.526 million) or more.

Experts disagree about the impact the tax changes will have on the city's booming property market. Prices for London property rose 16.3% last year, according to the Land Registry, which monitors British housing prices. In prime central London, where the buying frenzy has traditionally been the fiercest, the rate of growth has slowed since the surges of 2012 and 2013, though prices continue to rise. The prime neighborhoods of Kensington and Chelsea saw growth of 11.5% last year, compared with 12.1% in 2013. The less affluent but more affordable and up-and-coming east London borough of Waltham Forest saw a rise of 25.1% in the same period

Real-estate agent Robert Bailey says he fears the new capital gains tax will tarnish the London market in the eyes of international buyers. "One agent I spoke to in Monaco said that they are seeing a boom in foreigners who have been put off buying here because of the changes, going so far as to label Monaco 'London on Sea' thanks to all the defectors," he said.

Oliver Griffiths, of estate agents Jackson-Stops & Staff, is based in the affluent neighborhood of Richmond, west London. "The changes will throw cold water on the increasing London market because prime central London has historically been snapped up by international buyers and that has consequently pushed prices through the roof," he said.

"I have someone on my books from Saudi Arabia, who bought a GBP 2.25 million [$3.43 million] house on the river in Richmond and has rented it for years. She has had a five-year contract in place, but now wants to pay the contract out and sell up prior to April to avoid the new 28% tax."

However buying agent Edward Heaton, of Heaton and Partners, said he had only seen a "modest" increase in the number of international owners looking to sell and move on in order to avoid the new tax.

"The reality is that London remains an attractive destination and the cost of buying and selling are just something that people live with," he said.

Ed Tryon, director of Lichfields, a property-search agency, agrees that the allure of London outweighs the new tax.

"There are no barriers to property ownership in the U.K., and no significant annual charges. There isn't a residency requirement as there is in several countries," he said. "The U.K. is one of the easiest countries to acquire property, and long-term growth has outperformed almost every other major asset class."

Estate agent Simon Armitage, of the London property firm Druce, covers the high-end neighborhoods of South Kensington and Chelsea. He believes the tax will only seriously impact the market below GBP 2 million ($3 million). "Those buying over this price are generally sufficiently financially comfortable to not have to worry about the extra tax," he said.

Those who are not able to simply overlook tax increases will begin looking at entirely new areas of the city, suspects Martin Clegg, head of residential investment at Cluttons estate agents.

These territories are mainly on the fringes of prime London -- areas like Earls Court, just west of Knightsbridge and in the throes of a major regeneration; Ladbroke Grove, north of fashionable Notting Hill; and Rotherhithe in east London, which has the added benefit of being close to the city's modern financial district at Canary Wharf.

David Ruddock, associate director of Marsh & Parsons estate agents in Marylebone, central London, estimates that 70% of properties in the area are currently sold to overseas buyers. He doesn't think they will stay away but believes the increased costs might change the way they shop.

"International buyers still consider London to have lower entry and exit costs than other European cities, though I suspect that they may look to buy more, cheaper units rather than one-off, big-ticket properties," he said.

Real-Estate Mogul Scores With Blackstone Sale
By Peter Grant
912 words
12 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Scott Rechler bet big on Manhattan commercial property in 2009, jumping into the market at the depths of the financial crisis.

After watching prices climb to new heights, the third-generation real-estate mogul with strong New York political ties is taking some chips off the table.

Mr. Rechler has agreed to sell a roughly 50% stake in a portfolio of six New York office buildings to private-equity firm Blackstone Group LP in a deal that values the properties at $4 billion, according to people familiar with the matter. The properties include the landmark Starrett-Lehigh Building on Manhattan's West Side, as well as 340 Madison Ave., 620 Sixth Ave. and 1330 Sixth Ave.

Mr. Rechler's company, RXR Realty, will continue to manage the properties and plans to use the proceeds of the sale to return about all of its investors' equity. The deal's valuation means the investors have generated an annual return of 25% on their money, according to people familiar with the deal.

The deal is the latest reminder of the New York market's recovery from the financial crisis. An index of Midtown Manhattan office prices stands at a record, 8% above the precrisis peak in 2007, according to research firm Green Street Advisors. The index had fallen 55% from peak to trough.

At the same time, the sale raises the question of how much room there is for further price gains. Green Street, which in 2011 argued that real estate was a better buy than bonds, recently said prices had risen to the point that property was now fairly valued. The danger, according to Green Street analyst Andrew McCulloch, is that interest rates rise without inflation sufficient enough to push rents higher as well.

Mr. Rechler, 47 years old, said he believes the New York office market will keep expanding. He pointed out that he still is buying, and he and his investors are keeping a substantial stake in the portfolio Blackstone is purchasing. "We believe there's still a lot of room to run," he said.

For Blackstone, the biggest private-equity owner of real estate in the U.S., the deal represents a shifting strategy. The New York-based company has been known mostly for buying properties at sharp discounts to their prices before the financial crisis. This strategy has been to improve the buildings' cash flows and then sell to investors looking for steady and less-risky returns.

The RXR portfolio, by contrast, is part of Blackstone's push into deals that are in between such core investments and riskier properties. Since launching the strategy in early 2014, the firm has amassed more than $3.5 billion in what it calls "core plus" investments.

The properties Blackstone is buying from RXR, with a combined 5.3 million square feet of commercial space, have the potential to increase their cash flow. They are about 90% leased, and some tenants are paying below-market rents.

The two sides were advised in the deal by an Eastdil Secured team led by Douglas Harmon and Adam Spies.

Mr. Rechler has a reputation for good timing. In 2006, he sold his company at the time, Reckson Associates Realty Corp., to SL Green Realty Corp. for $4 billion. A year later, the market peaked and began a prolonged slide.

A fundraiser for Andrew Cuomo in his successful 2010 campaign for New York governor, Mr. Rechler in 2011 was named by Mr. Cuomo to be vice chairman of the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, a position he still holds.

The latest transaction will give Mr. Rechler capital for other projects.

Mr. Rechler is the third generation of one of the New York region's real-estate dynasties. The family started out focusing on Long Island industrial buildings in the 1950s and 1960s. Mr. Rechler recalls attending business meetings when he was 7 years old. He started working for the family business full time in 1989 after graduating with a joint degree in economics and political science from Clark University and a master's degree in real-estate finance from New York University.

Mr. Rechler became co-chief executive with his uncle at the age of 31. But there was friction between Mr. Rechler and other family members, according to Scott's brother, Gregg. In the early part of the last decade, family members decided to go separate ways and Reckson sold to Mr. Rechler's relatives its portfolio of Long Island industrial properties for about $316 million in cash and Reckson securities, leaving him in control of Reckson.

That turned out to be a smart move when SL Green bought Reckson after a bidding battle with billionaire investor Carl Icahn. The value of the properties Reckson owned fell sharply along with most commercial real estate after the 2008 crisis.

Mr. Rechler started looking for deals soon after the dust settled. He raised money from pension and sovereign-wealth funds to start buying distressed Manhattan assets, which he started doing in 2009. Last year, with values far higher, Mr. Rechler and his investors began talking to Blackstone.

"It's a dazzling deal," said Mary Ann Tighe, chief executive of the New York region for real-estate-services firm CBRE Group Inc. "It makes Scott's current investors delighted, replenishes his capital and gives him a powerful new partner."

U.S. News: Growth in Home Prices Accelerated At Year-End
By Kris Hudson
478 words
12 February 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
Copyright 2015, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Growth in home prices quickened at the end of last year -- depending on the region.

The median sale price of an existing home was $208,700 last quarter, up 6% from a year earlier, according to a report released Wednesday by the National Association of Realtors. That marked a quickening pace from the third quarter, when prices were up by 4.8% from a year earlier.

Of the 175 U.S. metro areas covered in the report, 86% registered increases in their median price on finalized sales in the fourth quarter from a year ago. In comparison, only 73% posted year-over-year gains in the third quarter. The biggest gains were seen in some of the areas hardest hit during the housing-market downturn.

Driving the gains: the supply of homes available for sale remained relatively tight and demand was stoked by the improving economy and low interest rates.

To wit, the supply of homes available for resale in the fourth quarter amounted to 4.9 months, meaning it would take that long to burn through that inventory at the current sales pace. That's roughly in line with the supply level of the comparable year-earlier period. However, it's short of the supply typically considered representative of a healthy market: six to seven months.

"The factors driving demand are pretty steady," said Danielle Hale, director of housing statistics for the Realtor association. "The fact that demand is outpacing supply means that we see some acceleration in prices. Mortgage rates were quite low in the fourth quarter, and that played a role."

Median prices increased most steeply in several cities in Nevada, Florida and Illinois that are rebounding from dramatic declines, as well as in smaller, booming cities in Texas. The biggest gain came north of Dallas, where the Sherman-Dennison area posted a 21.1% increase from a year ago to a median price of $128,200.

Many markets seeing the biggest price declines are in the Midwest and Northeast, led by South Bend, Ind., which was down 7.9%; Hartford, Conn., down 6.8%; Cape Girardeau, Mo., down 6.1%; Reading, Pa., down 5.5%; and Appleton, Wis., down 4.1%.

To be sure, some previously hot markets, namely in energy-rich Texas, likely are headed for a slowdown due to ripple effects of the steep decline in oil prices of recent months. On Wednesday, the Houston Association of Realtors reported that pending sales were down 9.3% to 3,382.

The faster pace of home appreciation runs counter to the expectation of many economists that appreciation would start to slow last year and this year as more supply hit the market. But that supply is taking longer to arrive than expected.

That's roughly in line with the supply level of the comparable year-earlier period. However, it's short of the supply typically considered representative of a healthy market: six to seven months.

Meanwhile, the volume of resales declined by 1% in the fourth quarter from the third to nearly 5.1 million homes.

The factors driving demand are pretty steady," said Danielle Hale, director of housing statistics for the Realtor association. The fact that demand is outpacing supply means that we see some acceleration in prices. Mortgage rates were quite low in the fourth quarter, and that played a role."

Median prices increased most steeply in several cities in Nevada, Florida and Illinois that are rebounding from dramatic declines, as well as in smaller, booming cities in Texas. The faster pace of home appreciation runs counter to the expectation of many economists that appreciation would start to slow last year and this year as more supply hit the market. But that supply is taking longer to arrive than expected.

The Realtors association has posited for months that the solution to the dearth of supply is more home construction. The 435,000 newly built homes that builders sold last year amounted to only 58% of their average annual output since 2000. That malaise might be lifting, since several big builders have reported in recent weeks that their sales have gained momentum since the fourth quarter.

In the Denver market, where homes listed for sale are scarce, Redfin real-estate agent Karla Kirkpatrick Adams said many homes are attracting multiple offers from would-be buyers. That's likely a factor in Denver area registering a 12.7% increase in its median resale price to $314,800 in the fourth quarter from a year ago.

I've had clients that were making offers on multiple houses and finding out that there were anywhere from five to 26 offers on every home that we bid on," Ms. Adams said last week.

In Seattle, Redfin agent Klaus Gosma held an open house on Jan. 24 and 25 for one of his listings, a three-bedroom home priced at $350,000. The open house attracted 132 visitors. The house, built in 2008, received five offers and now is under contract for more than the list price. Mr. Gosma attributed the house's popularity to a combination of low inventory and the price point being super attractive and affordable."

The U.S. housing market stumbled into 2015 as a leading indicator of home sales dipped in December.

The National Association of Realtors pending home sales index, which tracks contract signings for purchases of previously owned homes, dropped 3.7% in December from the prior month, its largest one-month decline in a year, the trade group said Thursday[http://www.realtor.org/news-releases/2015/01/pending-home-sales-stall-in-december].

Thursday's report indicates existing-home sales likely will decline in January, several economists said, a reversal from December's 2.4% gain[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/u-s-existing-home-sales-rose-in-december-1422025293].

The rebound in mortgage applications in recent weeks offers a degree of hope for the spring selling season, but overall housing looks moribund," Pantheon Macroeconomics chief economist Ian Shepherdson said in a note to clients.

Still, the pending-sales index rose 6.1% in December from a year earlier for its strongest annual gain since June 2013.

Lawrence Yun, the NAR's chief economist, said potential buyers last month faced rising prices and fewer choices as housing inventory declined from November. With interest rates at lows not seen since early 2013, the strength in existing sales in upcoming months will largely depend on the willingness of current homeowners to realize their equity gains from the past couple years and trade up," Mr. Yun said.

News Corp, owner of The Wall Street Journal, also owns Move Inc., which operates a website and mobile products for the National Association of Realtors.

The U.S. housing sector posted somewhat lackluster growth in 2014 as it struggled to regain its footing following a mid-2013 jump in mortgage rates.

Sales of previously owned homes, which make up roughly 90% of the U.S. market, strengthened in late 2014. But for the full year, sales fell 3.1% from 2013, according to NAR. Sales of new single-family homes[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/new-home-sales-rise-11-6-in-december-to-481-000-1422371230] picked up in December but rose just 1.2% for the year as a whole compared with 2013, according to the Commerce Department.

Through the volatility, the trend in home sales is up probably still up, albeit slowly," High Frequency Economics chief U.S. economist Jim O'Sullivan said in a note to clients.

Housing starts rose 8.8% last year[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/housing-starts-rise-4-4-in-december-1421847270], largely reflecting strong demand for apartment buildings and other multifamily structures, the Commerce Department said. Permits, a forward-looking indicator for residential construction, dipped 1.9% in December from the prior month.

Mortgage rates are low, potentially supporting home-buying activity in the spring. The average interest rate[http://www.freddiemac.com/pmms/index.html?intcmp=3DCWS-HP] on a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage is 3.66%, Freddie Mac said Thursday, down from an average of 4.43% in January 2014.

But a recent surge in mortgage applications[http://www.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/mortgage-applications-soar-as-consumers-snap-up-low-rates-1421236802] has primarily reflected homeowners looking to refinance, not new home purchases.

According to Thursday's report, pending home sales declined across the nation in December. The NAR gauge fell 2.6% from November in the South, declined 2.8% in the Midwest, dropped 4.6% in the West and tumbled 7.5% in the Northeast.

Even so, the index remained up from a year earlier in all four regions of the country.
