Commercial Property -- The Commish: Newmark Grubb Knight Frank Gets Sales Boost
By Peter Grant
720 words
8 December 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Newmark Grubb Knight Frank, known mostly for its strength in the commercial real estate leasing market, is about to become a bigger force in the sales world.

Newmark's parent company, BGC Partners Inc., is acquiring Apartment Realty Advisors, one of the largest brokers of apartment buildings in the U.S., for about $110 million.

The deal is the latest sign of BGC's effort to use its strong balance sheet to add muscle to New York-based Newmark and build it into one of the world's largest real-estate services firms.

BGC, a spinoff of Cantor Fitzgerald, also is buying Apartment Realty as the apartment-building market is booming. Values have been rising, along with rents and occupancies, during much of the downturn as living patterns have shifted in the U.S. and more people are opting to rent rather than own.

The Labor Department's report on Friday showed November was the strongest month of hiring since January 2012, providing another positive sign. The market is robust, said Barry Gosin, Newmark's chief executive. "People may not buy a home. But they'll rent."

Apartment Realty, which sold properties worth more than $10 billion in 2013, is a network of brokerage firms in 28 cities with about 15 different owners that share information, contacts and technology. BGC is essentially buying all those separate firms and putting them all under Newmark.

The deal was complicated for Mr. Gosin and James Kuhn, Newmark's president, to pull off because it involved numerous transactions. Mr. Gosin said it helped that Newmark's Denver operation, which Newmark acquired in 2013, has owned a large stake in the Denver office in the Apartment Realty network.

"It was kind of like living together before we got married," Mr. Gosin said.

Apartment Realty executives say they like the deal because BGC and Newmark have the capital markets expertise that buyers and sellers increasingly are demanding. Newmark also has operations throughout the world and will be able to extend the global reach of owners wanting to sell or refinance properties through Apartment Realty.

BGC and Newmark have a "heavy New York-centric capital markets expertise," said Blake Okland, chairman of ARA's executive committee. "We're happy to bolt on to that."

The commercial real estate business has seen an increasing number of mergers and acquisitions as firms have recognized the importance of scale. The price BGC is paying for Apartment Realty might rise beyond the $110 million if certain performance targets are met.

Newmark has been one of the world's fastest-growing commercial real estate firms since it was bought by BGC in 2011. Other acquisitions have included the assets of Grubb & Ellis Co. in 2012 and Cornish & Carey Commercial this year.

Newmark had $655 million in revenue for the 12 months that ended Sept. 30. Mr. Gosin said he hopes the firm's revenue will reach $1 billion in 2015, when the figure will include the recent Cornish & Carey and Apartment Realty acquisitions.

Newmark already is active in the sales market in a few regions, particularly New York, focusing on buildings valued at $50 million and less.

Buying Apartment Realty will greatly expand Newmark's contacts with the leading pension funds, private-equity firms and other institutional buyers of apartment buildings. Those same institutions are also active buyers of other types of commercial property, which could help Newmark's other sales businesses, Mr. Gosin said.

The risk in BGC's deal for Apartment Realty lies in a cooling off in the so-called multifamily market. Analysts say this could happen if the economy takes a turn for the worse or if home sales take off and the U.S. shifts back to more of a nation of owners rather than renters.

But such warnings have been around for more than two years and the apartment rental market has kept chugging. Rents have risen nationally for 23 straight quarters and are 15.2% higher than they were at the end of the recession in 2009, according to Reis Inc.

"Multifamily transaction volume should continue to be robust," said Dave Bragg, a Green Street Advisors analyst, in an email.

The Global 'Mansion Tax' Morass --- As New York, London and other major cities consider new taxes on wealthy home buyers, experts debate how levies impact the real-estate market
By Eliot Brown and Art Patnaude
714 words
8 December 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
27
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Luxury-home prices in 33 major cities around the world -- from Los Angeles to Monaco -- are up 36% since 2009, according to data from real-estate firm Knight Frank.

The taxman has noticed.

To bolster public coffers or to curb rampant growth, a number of cities, including Singapore, Hong Kong and San Francisco, have imposed or increased taxes on wealthy home buyers in recent years. In other major cities, such as New York, London and Paris, new taxes on high-end homes and second homes are on the table.

Economists and real estate professionals are studying the effect of so-called mansion taxes on the property market, and so far their findings are mixed. Hong Kong, for example, levied a tax on foreign investors in 2012 and increased a transfer tax on expensive homes in 2013. Since then, transaction volume has fallen to about 8,000 home sales a month from more than 12,000 in 2011, according to research firm Colliers International. But home prices have proven resilient. While they initially fell about 5%, they have bounced back to record levels.

Taxing the rich via their property is hardly a new concept. In 17th-century England, King William III taxed property owners based on the number of windows in their houses, since the wealthy tended to have larger homes with more windows. The market reacted: To avoid the tax, builders included fewer and fewer windows -- and some houses had rooms without windows at all, according to a paper published in April by the Lincoln Institute of Land Policy.

In the modern era, such taxes have been relatively infrequent, with governments preferring annual taxes based on assessed value. In Germany and Finland, for example, wealth taxes were largely abandoned in the past two decades.

In implementing a mansion tax, the methods vary, as do the rationales. Both Hong Kong and Singapore increased taxes on foreign investors buying property to intentionally deflate prices in a real estate bubble created by a flood of purchases by Chinese investors.

In the U.K., a proposed mansion tax stands to be a focal point of the national election in May. The Labour Party, vying for power against the current Conservative-led coalition government, has vowed to introduce a levy on homes worth above GBP 2 million, or about $3.1 million, in an appeal to working-class voters.

Many high-end brokers and would-be buyers say that the tax would stifle the market. Notably, Angelina Jolie, visiting to promote a new film last month, said in a televised interview that a mansion tax could affect her decision-making. "I'm quite reasonable about money," Ms. Jolie said. "That could put me off."

Critics say the mansion tax would have the greatest impact on buyers within the U.K. "For ultrahigh net worth individuals who can buy anywhere in the world, it's not going to stop them," said Shirley Humphrey, director at Harrods Estates, the estate-agency arm of the luxury department store in London. "It would have more of an effect on the local U.K. market. For instance, it could stop people from moving up to that next property on the ladder," she said.

Economists, meanwhile, generally believe such taxes are a blunt instrument and say that similar goals can be achieved by income taxes or increasing property taxes.

Wojciech Kopczuk, an economist at New York's Columbia University who has studied mansion taxes, said the transfer taxes that have been proposed and implemented are "distortive," causing some sellers to rethink a sale at all and thus slowing down the market. "Transaction taxes stop people from trading," he said.

Still, Mr. Kopczuk said, depending on the motivations of those implementing the tax, such measures tend to be effective in redistributing some wealth given that many rich homeowners often live and pay taxes elsewhere. "If I want to give them credit for the point of view of economics," he said, "these are people that you cannot otherwise tax with other forms of taxation."

Dodgy Home Appraisals Stage a Comeback in U.S.
By AnnaMaria Andriotis
766 words
3 December 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Home appraisers are inflating the values of some properties they assess, often at the behest of loan officers and real-estate agents, in what industry executives say is a return to practices seen before the financial crisis.

An estimated one in seven appraisals conducted from 2011 through early 2014 inflated home values by 20% or more, according to data provided to The Wall Street Journal by Digital Risk Analytics, a subsidiary of Digital Risk LLC.

The mortgage-analysis and consulting firm based in Maitland, Fla., was hired by some of the 20 largest lenders to review their loan files.

The firm reviewed more than 200,000 mortgages, parsing the homes' appraised values and other information, including the properties' sizes and similar homes sold in the areas at the times. The review was conducted using the firm's software and staff appraisers.

Bankers, appraisers and federal officials in interviews said inflated appraisals are becoming more widespread as the recovery in the housing market cools.

While home prices are increasing generally, their appreciation is slowing, and sales have been weak despite low interest rates.

The dollar amount of new mortgages issued this year is expected to be down 39% from last year, at about $1.12 trillion, according to the Mortgage Bankers Association.

That has put increasing pressure on loan officers, who depend on originating new mortgages for their income, as well as real-estate agents, who live on sales commissions. That in turn is raising the heat on appraisers, whose valuations can make or break a sale. Banks generally won't agree to a mortgage if the purchase price or the refinancing amount is higher than the appraised value.

The practice is garnering broader notice. The Office of the Comptroller of the Currency is reviewing the mortgages banks are doling out, concerned that some of them are based on inflated values, according to Darrin Benhart, a deputy comptroller who focuses on identifying areas of risk in the federal banking system. The OCC oversees national bank and federal savings associations lending practices. Separately, Freddie Mac, the mortgage-finance giant, said it has opened fraud investigations related to appraisals of homes backing mortgages it bought.

Almost 40% of appraisers surveyed from Sept. 15 through Nov. 7 reported experiencing pressure to inflate values, according to Allterra Group LLC, a for-profit appraiser-advocacy firm based in Salisbury, Md. That figure was 37% in the survey for the previous year.

"If you thought what was happening before was an embarrassment, wait until the second time around," said Joan Trice, Allterra's chief executive and founder of the Collateral Risk Network, which represents appraisers employed by lenders and other companies and has been meeting with regulators to discuss concerns about appraisers being pressured into inflating values.

To determine a home's value, appraisers assess a property's condition, size and location, among other factors. They use similar properties that have been recently sold as one key basis for comparison.

Digital Risk found that some appraised values were off the mark based on discrepancies that appeared unintentional, though, "at other times, the appraiser's selection of [comparable properties] . . . is very hard to justify," said Thomas Showalter, chief analytics officer at Digital Risk. The firm saw cases where values for decades-old homes were determined based on sales prices for newly constructed ones, and homes blocks from shorelines were compared with waterfront properties, he said.

To be sure, inflated appraisals aren't a problem everywhere. Real-estate agents, for example, say they sometimes encounter appraisals that come in below the agreed-upon purchase price and derail the deal.

Twenty-four percent of real-estate agents surveyed in March 2014 reported that low appraisals resulted in sales contracts being canceled, delayed or negotiated to a lower price, according to the latest data from the National Association of Realtors. But that figure has been declining in recent years: In March 2013, 29% of agents reported such problems, and the figure was 31% in March 2012.

Brandon Boudreau, chief operating officer at Metro-West Appraisal Co. LLC, a national firm based in Detroit, says he and his appraisers often feel pressured by aggressive real-estate agents.

"It's a daily thing," Mr. Boudreau says. "They say, 'Your appraiser is incompetent. They don't know what they're doing.'" In such instances, Mr. Boudreau says he tells the real-estate agents to contact the loan officer handling the borrower's application with evidence the appraiser undervalued the home.

'Black Friday' Sales Boom Fades for U.S. Retailers
By Shelly Banjo
841 words
2 December 2014
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
23
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Retail spending over the U.S. Thanksgiving weekend fell 11%, according to the main industry trade group, a sign that the annual four-day shopping bonanza may be losing some of its punch.

The decline, the second annual drop in a row, came in part because retailers started offering deals in the days and even weeks before, giving U.S. shoppers ample opportunities to snag discounts without having to endure the lines and crowds that typify Black Friday.

Target Corp., for instance, offered its first round of so-called Black Friday deals on Nov. 10, while Wal-Mart Stores Inc. kicked off its holiday promotions at the start of the month.

Total spending from Thursday through Sunday sank 11% from a year earlier to $50.9 billion, according to the National Retail Federation, which surveyed 4,600 consumers on Friday and Saturday.

Shoppers spent an average of $380.95, down 6.4% from a year earlier. The trade group still expects total holiday sales through the end of the year to rise 4.1%.

Shoppers "don't feel like they have to get out that one day or miss spending the holidays with their families," said Elissa Margolis, senior vice president for Disney Store North America at Walt Disney Co. The company, which operates more than 200 Disney stores in North America, began offering promotions on items like dolls and costumes from the "Frozen" and "Star Wars" movies online and in its stores on the Monday before Thanksgiving.

This year, brick-and-mortar retailers have stepped up their Web offerings with more promotions like free shipping, rolling sales through the month and price-matching online rivals such as Amazon.com Inc. The more robust Web selection and discounts have allowed shoppers to take advantage of Black Friday discounts without interrupting time at home.

The retail trade group said the number of people who went shopping over the four-day weekend declined 5.2% from last year to 134 million.

The weak results contrast with improvements elsewhere in the economy, such as decreasing joblessness, a rebounding housing market and two quarters of strong economic growth, and they suggest that even with an economy on the mend many consumers remain thrifty five years after the recession. Consumers have become much more dutiful about researching their purchases on the Web to find the best price and have also become more disciplined, limiting their impulse buys.

Barbara Holmes, a 37-year-old pharmacy technician from Trenton, N.J., went to RadioShack in Morrisville, Pa., to buy her daughter a new LG phone priced at $39.99. She went only for the one deal and planned to shop online for other needs, like clothing. Ms. Holmes shopped at Wal-Mart last year but was skipping the crowds and long lines this year. "I ain't going up there with all the crazy people," she said.

The sales drop this weekend could sting retailers, which had been generating a 10th of their holiday sales over that weekend, but it doesn't mean retailers are going to give up the annual shopping ritual anytime soon.

"Finishing Thanksgiving dinner and offering sales is like ringing the bell that alerts people it's gift-giving season, and that will always be the case," said Steve Davis, CEO of Rue La La, a fashion discounter that is majority owned by Kynetic LLC.

For others, it's a matter of staying in the game alongside the big retailers that spend heavily on promotions.

"It's really important for us to stay out there at all times to drive business, even if that means starting the sales earlier and earlier," said Jon Abt, co-president of Abt Electronics & Appliances in Glenview, Ill., which takes in a quarter of its sales online. Sales over Thursday, Friday and Saturday rose 40% online and 20% in the store from the year before, he said.

Many retailers offered the same discounts on the Web as they offered in stores. The Internet didn't attract more shoppers or more spending than last year, according to NRF's closely watched survey. Online shopping accounted for 42% of spending over the four-day period, down from 44% last year, the trade group said, with the average amount consumers spent online declining 10% from a year ago to $159.55.

The survey, conducted by a third party Friday and Saturday, polled 4,631 consumers. Other forecasters came up with slightly different results.

ShopperTrak, which monitors shopper visits at tens of thousands of brick-and-mortar sites, said sales over Thursday and Friday slipped 0.5%. Data from International Business Machines Corp., based on its survey of online transactions at 800 retail websites, showed online sales rose more slowly on Thanksgiving and Black Friday than they did last year.

Holiday-shopping figures often differ as forecasters use different methodologies to collect different sets of data.

---

U.S. News: New-Home Sales Are Still Sluggish
By Eric Morath
450 words
28 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. housing market was supposed to give the economy a big boost this year. It isn't turning out that way.

While sales of previously owned homes have rebounded after a weak start to the year, new-home sales are barely trending above last year's levels and home construction has been flat despite low interest rates and a healing labor market.

Gross domestic product expanded 2.4% in the third quarter from a year earlier, driven largely by better consumer and business spending. Residential investment, though, a measure of home building and improvements, was down 0.8% from a year earlier.

"Consumers continue to face many of the same lingering barriers, including minimal income growth, difficulty accessing credit and dilapidated savings," said Sterne Agee economist Lindsey Piegza. Those factors restrict "consumers' ability to purchase large-ticket items such as a new home."

Sales of new single-family homes rose only slightly in October after the three previous months of data were revised lower, the Commerce Department said Wednesday. October's seasonally adjusted annual pace of 458,000 remained just below a postrecession peak reached in June 2013 and is roughly half the average annual level recorded from 1996 through 2006.

In the first 10 months, sales rose 1% from the same period in 2013. New homes represent only about 10% of all sales, but construction adds more jobs and investment into the economy compared with purchasing a previously owned house.

Rising prices are putting new homes out of reach for some buyers. In October, the median price for a new home rose 15% from a year earlier to $305,000, the highest level on record.

David Weekley Homes Chief Executive John Johnson said further improvements in the economy and consumer confidence are needed. "I think we saw a little price resistance from the consumer," he said. "There are a bunch of people who are waiting until they feel more confident."

The unemployment rate sank to the lowest level since 2008 last month, and the average interest rate on a 30-year, fixed-rate mortgage hovers near 4%. But mortgages remain difficult for many Americans to obtain, and there is relatively weak demand for single-family houses among younger adults.

"Millennials are still at the stage of moving out of their parents' homes and renting, and those who lost homes in foreclosures still can't qualify for credit," said Jed Kolko, economist for the real-estate website Trulia. "I'd be surprised if we saw a dramatic improvement in the next year."

Dow, S&P 500 Close at Record Levels; Stock Moves Muted in Quiet Trading Before Thanksgiving
By Saumya Vaishampayan
808 words
26 November 2014
16:54
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The Dow industrials and S&P 500 ended at record levels Wednesday in a quiet, preholiday trading session.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 12.81 points, or 0.1%, to 17827.75. The blue-chip index pushed into positive territory in the last hour of trading, notching its 30th record close for the year. The S&P 500 index gained 5.8 points, or 0.3%, to 2072.83, marking its 47th closing high in 2014.

The Nasdaq Composite Index gained 29.07 points, or 0.6%, to 4787.32. The U.S. stock market will be closed Thursday for the Thanksgiving holiday.

While several economic reports missed expectations, investors said they were still consistent with an economic recovery in the U.S.

Improving U.S. economic data and upbeat corporate earnings have helped propel U.S. stocks to a series of record highs in recent weeks. Data released this week showed the U.S. economy grew at a pace of 3.9% in the third quarter, higher than the initial estimate of 3.5%. The growth rate in the second and third quarters combined marked the best six-month pace since the second half of 2003.

The U.S. economy is progressing nicely, said Burt White, chief investment officer at broker-dealer LPL Financial. We dont see the data today derailing that view.

Investors looked ahead to a Thursday meeting of the Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries, which takes place amid a backdrop of sharply lower oil prices. Saudi Arabias oil minister on Wednesday hinted he wouldnt push for a cut in OPECs production targets. Poorer countries have advocated for production cuts to boost oil prices.

Crude-oil futures fell 0.5% to $73.69 a barrel. In other commodity markets, gold futures fell less than 0.1% to $1,196.60 an ounce.

Deep-water drilling companies fell the most on the S&P. Diamond Offshore Drilling Inc. shares slumped 11% and those of Transocean Ltd. fell 8.1%.

While lower oil prices will hit shares of energy companies, many investors say the slide in oil could eventually boost consumer spending. That would spill over into increased earnings for U.S. companies, especially in the consumer discretionary space, and help lift stocks.

Consumer spending is key to drive the equity market higher, said Terry Sandven, chief equity strategist at U.S. Bank Wealth Management. Robust consumer spending has yet to materialize, but Mr. Sandven said hes watching for sales data from the Thanksgiving weekend to see if consumers are more willing to spend.

Wednesdays U.S. economic data included two weak readings on home sales. New home sales rose 0.7% in October to an annual rate of 458,000, the Commerce Department said. Economists in a Wall Street Journal survey had expected a pace of 470,000 last month. Separately, an index of pending sales of existing homes unexpectedly fell.

The housing market continues to be the only question mark in the U.S. recovery, said Omar Aguilar, chief investment officer of equities at Charles Schwab Investment Management.

Jobless claims rose 21,000 to 313,000 in the week ended Nov. 22, the Labor Department said. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had expected 289,000 claims. Still, claims remain near postrecession lows, a sign that the labor market continues to recover.

U.S. consumer spending rose 0.2% in October from the prior month, slightly less than the 0.3% expected by economists. The Feds preferred gauge of inflation, the price index for personal consumption expenditures, rose 0.1% and is up 1.4% from a year earlier. That is the 30th month in a row that inflation has stayed below the Feds 2% annual target.

Meanwhile, orders for durable goods unexpectedly rose 0.4% in October from a month earlier. Orders were boosted by a rise in military spending that masked weak demand for other goods.

Europe continues to struggle with dangerously low inflation and tepid growth. European Central Bank Vice President Vitor Constancio on Wednesday suggested the bank is ready to buy government bonds early next year if more-aggressive stimulus measures are needed. German government bonds rose, nearing all-time highs, in the wake of the remarks. The Stoxx Europe 600 ended flat.

The yield on the 10-year Treasury note fell to 2.234%. Yields fall as prices rise.

In corporate news, Deere Co. forecast a 20% decline in sales of farm equipment for the recently started business year. The gloomy projection overshadowed quarterly results that topped Wall Streets expectations. Shares fell 0.9%.

SNAPSHOT

U.S. stocks ended slightly higher in quiet, pre-holiday trading, with the Dow and S&P 500 setting new records, despite a batch of lackluster U.S. economic data. Treasurys got a boost from the soft numbers, while the dollar fell as investors booked profits. Oil prices finished at four-year lows after the Saudi oil minister hinted he wouldnt push for a cut in OPECs production targets. Gold settled flat.

OPENING CALL

OPEC will be in the spotlight Thursday. The Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries is on the brink of a decision that could send oil prices up--or keep them depressed. While prices have been falling since midsummer, OPEC has done nothing to intervene. Speculation has been rife as to why. A reduction of 1.5 million barrels a day is needed to tighten oil markets next year, according to oil analysts at Barclays. After remarks from Saudis oil minister on Wednesday, John Kilduff, founding partner of Again Capital in New York, said he thinks its pretty clear coming out of these meetings that there will not be a production cut.

EQUITIES

The Dow industrials and S&P 500 ended at record highs in a quiet, preholiday trading session.

In Asian trading Wednesday, China shares climbed as last weeks interest-rate cut continued to reverberate through the market, giving Shanghai its fifth-straight session of gains. Elsewhere in the region, stock markets were mixed.

FOREX

The dollar weakened against the yen and the euro after new data showing the U.S. economy is improving modestly underscored expectations the Federal Reserve wont raise interest rates until later next year.

The U.S. data showed inflation ticked up last month as consumer spending rose, weekly jobless claims picked up, and business investment weakened in October as military spending increased. Collectively, the numbers pointed to a U.S. economy that is gaining traction slowly.

The dollar weakened for a second straight session despite signs of rising inflation and increasing growth over the past two days, two elements the Federal Reserve has said must precede higher interest rates. Expectations of higher U.S. rates and signs of looser monetary policy abroad have lifted the greenback against its rivals over the past few weeks.

Lower consumer confidence and higher jobless claims, alongside the weeks Thanksgiving holiday and the coming month end, presented an opportunity for investors to close up some bets on a stronger dollar, said Omer Esiner, chief market analyst at the currency brokerage Commonwealth Foreign Exchange Inc.

The data were a good excuse to book some profits on the dollars rally over the last few weeks, Mr. Esiner said.

BONDS

U.S. government bonds strengthened for a fifth straight session as a string of disappointing U.S. economic releases boosted demand for haven assets.

U.S. bond markets will be shut Thursday for Thanksgiving and will close early on Friday.

Jobless claims rose to an 11-week high in the latest week, a setback for a labor market that has recently gained traction. Personal spending posted a smaller pace of increase than economists had forecast. Pending new home sales unexpectedly declined.

Todays data reflected a broad-based softening in the U.S. economy in the fourth quarter, after a relatively strong performance in the third quarter, said James DeMasi, chief fixed-income strategist at Stifel Nicolaus in Baltimore. With economic momentum fading, inflation will likely continue to slip further away from the Feds 2.0% target rate over the coming months, which will keep the Fed from raising rates until late 2015 at the earliest.

U.S. inflation remained below the Federal Reserves annual 2% target for the 30th consecutive month in October, giving the central bank some breathing room in moving toward higher borrowing costs for the economy.

The price index for personal consumption expenditures, the Feds preferred inflation measure, rose 0.1% from September and is up 1.4% from a year earlier, the Commerce Department said.

A $29 billion sale of seven-year notes drew strong demand, wrapping up this weeks strong auctions of new U.S. government debt.

The new seven-year notes were sold at a yield of 1.96%, the lowest since October 2013. The auction drew $2.63 bids for each dollar offered, the highest since February. Indirect bidding, a proxy gauge of demand from foreign investors, jumped to 50%, the highest since 2011.

The bond market was also supported by month-end demand from fund managers. Some investors bought bonds Wednesday because they want to avoid thin liquidity on Friday.

COMMODITIES

Oil prices sagged to four-year lows, as Saudi Arabias oil minister hinted he wouldnt push for a cut in OPECs production targets.

The Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries is gathering for its most closely watched meeting in years, and observers are growing pessimistic about the cartel making any big moves to stop the 30% slide in prices since the summer.

Saudi Oil Minister Ali al-Naimi said that he expected the market to stabilize itself eventually, breaking his silence on the state of the market.

I think its pretty clear coming out of these meetings that there will not be a production cut, said John Kilduff, founding partner of Again Capital in New York, which invests in energy commodities. The Saudis have prevailed in making it clear that the only people theyd be helping with a production cut is the competition--U.S. shale producers, Mr. Kilduff said.

U.S. shale-oil producers need oil prices in the range of $53 to $78 a barrel to break even, depending on their circumstances, meaning most are profitable even with prices at current levels, according to estimates presented at an OPEC technical meeting last week.

Gold prices settled barely changed in quiet, preholiday trading as investors found little direction in lackluster U.S. economic data and a flat stock market.

U.S. Pending Home Sales Fall 1.1% in October; New Sign of an Uneven Housing Recovery
By Jonathan House
578 words
26 November 2014
11:53
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

WASHINGTONThe number of contracts signed to buy previously owned homes fell in October, a new sign of an uneven housing recovery.

An index of pending sales of existing homes, reflecting purchases under contract but not yet closed, fell 1.1% to 104.1 in October from September, the National Association of Realtors said Wednesday. It was up 2.2% from the same period a year earlier.

The index has been inching higher this year and is above the 100 level the trade group defines as an average level of activity. But it has struggled to regain the postrecession highs it hit in mid-2013 before rising interest rates dented the housing recovery.

Ian Shepherdson, chief economist at Pantheon Macroeconomics, said the report provided yet more evidence that the trend in housing-market activity is flat.

Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had forecast a 0.5% rise in October sales.

News Corp, owner of The Wall Street Journal, also owns Move Inc., which operates a website and mobile products for the National Association of Realtors.

The sales tallied for the pending-sales index typically close a month or two later. The National Association of Realtors closely watched barometer of existing-home sales, which measures sales after contracts close, rose 1.5% in October to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 5.26 million.

That was the highest level in a year for sales of existing homes, which account for around 90% of all transactions in the market. But Octobers decline in the pending sales suggests the gauge of existing home sales will fall in the months ahead.

Sales of newly built single-family homes[http://online.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/new-home-sales-rose-in-october-september-number-revised-down-1417014453?KEYWORDS=new home sales] in the U.S. inched 0.7% higher in October from the prior month to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 458,000, according to a separate report from the Commerce Department Wednesday.

New home sales have largely disappointed this year. In the decade leading up to the recession, nearly 1 million new homes were sold on average annually.

Steady job growth, slowing price gains and an increased supply of homes for sale have helped to support demand for housing. U.S. employers added 214,000 jobs to payrolls last month[http://online.wsj.com.proxy.library.brocku.ca/articles/jobs-report-economy-adds-214000-jobs-1415367269] and are on pace to post the best yearly gain in employment since 1999.

Mortgage rates, which rose last year when the Federal Reserve signaled it would start rolling back its easy money policies, have been easing.

For the week ended late Thursday, the 30-year fixed-rate mortgage averaged 3.99%, according to data from Freddie Mac. That was down from 4.01% a week earlier and 4.22% a year earlier.

But a steady run-up in prices means housing affordability has continued to deteriorate. The mortgage payment for a median-priced U.S. home rose to 16.3% of median household income in June, a six-year high, according to a recent report from the National Association of Realtors.

The housing sector faces other headwinds including stagnant household incomes and banks that are still cautious about lending. Demand is holding steady but would be more robust if it werent for lagging wage growth and tight credit conditions, National Association of Realtors chief economist Lawrence Yun said.

WSJO
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The yearly growth in home prices across the U.S. decelerated further in September, according to a home price report released Tuesday.

The home price index covering the entire nation increased 4.8% in the 12 months ended in September, said the S&P/Case-Shiller Home Price Index report. That is down from 5.1% in August.

The home price index covering 10 major U.S. cities increased 4.8% in the year ended in September. The 20-city price index was up 4.9%. That is down from 5.6% in August but slightly above the 4.8% expected by economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal.

On an unadjusted basis, the 10-city and 20-city gauges were unchanged in September from August, while the national index slipped 0.1%. S&P said that was the first national decline since November 2013.

Seasonally adjusted, the U.S. index increased 0.7% in September, while the two city composites each rose 0.3%.

The slowdown reflects the lackluster pace of home demand as buyers are constrained by stagnant pay growth and tight lending conditions as well as lingering financial problems for some households even years after the recession.

The overall trend in home price increases continues to slow down, said David M. Blitzer, Chairman of the Index Committee at S&PDow Jones Indices.

Other housing statistics paint a mixed to slightly positive picture, he said, pointing to increased housing starts and a reduced mortgage default rate. With the economy looking better than a year ago, the housing outlook for 2015 is stable to slightly better.

Regionally, Charlotte and Dallas were the only cities to see their annual gains accelerate while Cleveland remained virtually unchanged for the fourth consecutive month.

Government Bonds in U.S., Europe Strengthen on China, ECB; Markets Impacted by Chinas Interest-Rate Cut, Eurozone Speculation
By Min Zeng
727 words
21 November 2014
15:32
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Prices of government bonds in the U.S. and Europe strengthened on Friday as bond investors cheered the latest signals from central banks in China and the eurozone to support the flagging economic growth.

In late-afternoon trading, the benchmark 10-year Treasury note was 5/32 higher, yielding 2.317%. Yields fall as prices rise.

In Europe, Greeces government bonds led the price rally Friday with the yield on the 10-year Greek government bond dropping over 0.3 percentage point to 7.981%. The yield on the 10-year German government bond fell to 0.771%. The yield on the 10-year U.K. government debt declined to 2.048%.

Chinas central bank announced Friday to lower interest rates for the first time in more than two years. The European Central Bank started buying asset-backed securities Friday. ECB President Mario Draghi said Friday that the bank is ready to take fresh monetary stimulus, boosting optimism that it would buy government bonds in the eurozone.

Central bank actions are putting downward pressure on global rates, said Andrew Hollenhorst, interest-rate strategist at Citigroup Global Markets Inc. in New York. It is just more evidence of central banks being willing to react to slowing growth with accommodative monetary policy.

The news came as global policy makers and investors confront an uneven pace of the global economic growth, lower energy prices and alarming low inflation in the eurozone. While the Federal Reserve has stopped buying bonds amid an improving U.S. economy, other major central banks including the ECB and the Bank of Japan have stepped up stimulus this year.

Major central banks have cut interest rates to record lows and embarked on unconventional easing measures such as buying government bonds following the 2008 financial crisis. The generous liquidity has kept global bond yields at very low levels and propped up prices of many riskier assets such as stocks, junk bonds and commodities.

The news out of China and the eurozone boosted investors appetites for riskier assets. The preference initially sapped demand for ultrasafe Treasury bonds, pushing the 10-year Treasury yield higher to 2.354% earlier Friday.

But buyers stepped in as Mr. Draghis comments raised speculation that the next step for the ECB would be to buy government bonds which sent yields in the eurozone falling broadly. U.S. bonds offer much more attractive yields compared with many developed bond markets. The rising dollar has added to the allure of U.S. financial assets to foreign buyers.

U.S. bonds continue to be attractive against the backdrop of low global bond yields, said James DeMasi, chief fixed-income strategist at Stifel Nicolaus Co. in Baltimore.

The uneven pace of the global economy has drawn investors into U.S. government bonds this year even as the U.S. economy has gained traction and the Fed wound down its monthly bond buying.

The 10-year Treasury yield has fallen from 3% at the start of January. The yield has traded in a tight band of 2.27% and 2.4% in November.

It is tough to bet on higher yields in the Treasury market at the moment, said Jason Rogan, managing director of U.S. government bond trading in New York at Guggenheim Securities LLC.

This year through Thursday, Treasury bonds overall have handed investors a total returnincluding price changes and interest paymentsof 4.19%, according to Barclays. Treasury bonds maturing in 10 years or longer have returned 18.6%.

On Thursday, a survey showed that manufacturing and services activity in the eurozone fell to a 16-month low in November. In China, the preliminary HSBC China manufacturing purchasing managers index fell to 50.0, the lowest reading since May. But U.S. existing home sales rose by 1.5% last month, compared with economists forecast of a 0.1% decline.

Fed officials have signaled patience in raising official interest rates from near zero amid contained inflation and mixed global growth.

Many investors expect the Fed to start raising rates in the second half of 2015, which has bolstered investors confidence that bond yields wouldn't rise sharply.

U.S. News: Housing Starts Fall, but Not in Key Segment
By Eric Morath and Kris Hudson
285 words
20 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Builders started construction on fewer properties last month, but a solid gain for single-family homes suggests the market may be strengthening heading into next year.

Housing starts fell 2.8% in October from a month earlier to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 1.009 million units, the Commerce Department said Wednesday. But the decline was driven by a slide in multifamily properties.

New construction on single-family units, roughly two-thirds of the market, rose 4.2% in October to reach its best pace since November 2013. Through the first 10 months of 2014, the number of single-family homes that builders started is up 5.3%, compared with the same period in 2013. That is a modest improvement, not the rapid acceleration some economists had projected would stir stronger growth.

The "gradual upward trend in housing starts" is supported by single-family building, Barclays economist Blerina Uruci said. "We maintain our view that housing activity continues to recover, although the pace of the recovery remains slower than in the previous couple of years."

Building permits, a bellwether of future construction, increased 4.8% last month to a 1.08 million rate, the best pace since June 2008. The recent growth in permitting, however, has been driven by multifamily properties. Individual units in that category tend to contribute less to employment and economic growth.

The number of houses builders started has slowly rebounded since the recession ended in mid-2009, but the pace remains well off from the annual average of 1.7 million from 1996 to 2006.

World News: U.S. Builders Are Hopeful, in All Regions
By Ben Leubsdorf
527 words
20 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ

English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

A gauge of home-builder sentiment rose in November -- with the first sign of optimism in the Northeast region since 2006 -- a hopeful signal for the U.S. housing recovery.

The housing-market index for new single-family homes rose four points to a seasonally adjusted level of 58 this month, the National Association of Home Builders said Tuesday.

A reading over 50 means most builders generally see conditions as positive.

The index has now been above the 50-point mark for five consecutive months, said David Crowe, the home-builder group's chief economist. "Low interest rates, affordable home prices and solid job creation are contributing to a steady housing recovery," he said. Now, economists are looking to see how the optimism plays out in terms of single-family housing starts, which have remained tepid over the past year.

While housing starts are up this year, construction of apartment buildings and other multifamily dwellings has outpaced the building of new single-family homes.

Construction this year through September rose 22.7% on structures with five or more units, compared with the first nine months of 2013. Single-family-home construction rose 3.8% from a year earlier, according to the Commerce Department.

"The key question now is if single-family starts are on their way to a rate seen in the past when the home-builder survey was registering readings similar to current ones," MFR Inc. chief U.S. economist Joshua Shapiro said in a note to clients. He doubts it, "given a considerable supply overhang of existing homes that is presenting significant competition to the new home market and likely will for the foreseeable future."

Instead, Mr. Shapiro said, "we expect a very slow rise in single-family starts going forward, with the earlier jump representing a move back toward more sustainable levels after activity plunged to rock-bottom post-bubble lows."

Beazer Homes USA Inc. Chief Executive Allan Merrill lends credence to the caution. "Despite favorable demographics and excellent affordability, demand for new homes has been uneven and, over the course of the year, somewhat disappointing," Mr. Merrill told analysts last week.

The NAHB sentiment gauge rose in all four regions of the country in November from the prior month. In the Northeast it hit 51, the first time the region has seen a reading of 50 or above since April 2006.

It remains to be seen if the Northeast's upward trend will hold in the coming months. The three-month moving average for the region rose to 44 in November from 41 in October, reaching its highest level since June 2006 but remaining well below the 50-point mark.

Starts on new single-family homes declined 5.5% in the Northeast during the first nine months of 2014 compared with the same period a year earlier, according to the Commerce Department. But construction of multifamily dwellings, like apartment buildings, has been much more robust. Overall, housing starts in the region are up 18.1% so far this year compared with 2013.

City Eyes Mansion-Tax Boost to Assist Affordable Housing; Soaring Luxury Real-Estate Prices May Bring Effort to Raise Tax
By Laura Kusisto and Eliot Brown

City Hall is considering a proposal that could take advantage of a soaring luxury real-estate market to help deliver on Mayor Bill de Blasios $41 billion affordable-housing plan by raising the tax on top-priced apartment sales, according to people familiar with the matter.

The plan, which would direct the additional money to affordable housing, would require the approval of the state Legislature. It is unclear just how seriously the de Blasio administration is considering a push for it in a legislative session shaping up to include several fights on real-estate policy.

But city officials have floated the idea with real-estate executives. While some of them oppose the notion as discriminatory against high-end buyers and an unreliable way to raise revenue, a handful of influential developers have been supportive of the concept, according to real-estate executives.

The supporters include Stephen Ross, chairman of Related Cos., the citys most prolific developer. While most active in the luxury and office segment, Mr. Ross has been pushing the city to fund more so-called workforce housing aimed at middle-income families.

He and other Related executives have told other developers they believed an increase in the so-called mansion taxwhich levies a 1% state tax on housing sales over $1 millionwould be a good source of money given the strength of the ultraluxury apartment market.

Others said they would support such a planparticularly as an alternative to other proposals being advocated by some tenant groups, including an annual tax on second homes or rescinding a tax break that encourages rental developments.

A tax on very high-end condos would be a better way to create resources for affordable housing than further taxing rental development, said Jed Walentas, who is redeveloping the site of the Domino Sugar factory in Brooklyn with more than 2,000 apartments.

Although the details of any increase to be proposed are unclear, the citys Independent Budget Office ran the numbers on a similar proposal to levy an additional 0.5% tax on apartment sales of $5 million or more that it said would generate $34 million in 2015, increasing in subsequent years.

The current 1% mansion tax generated $259 million for the state in fiscal year 2013, according to the New York State Division of Budget. That is up significantly from the real-estate slump, when the tax generated $176 million for the state in fiscal 2010.

Real-estate executives said that any successful proposal would likely be more complex than a simple, broad-based increase in the mansion tax and the additional proceeds would go to the city. For example, it might impose a higher tax on top-level salessay, at least $10 millionthan on lower-priced sales.

When asked about the administrations Albany agenda at a hearing Monday, Housing Preservation and Development Department Commissioner Vicki Been said the city planned to push for changes to the 421-a city tax break for new development and the J-51 tax break for renovating apartment buildings; strengthening the citys rent laws; and changing certain tax exemptions. She didnt mention the mansion tax.

A spokesman for the mayors office said, We are still developing our legislative agenda for the coming session. We fully intend to work with our partners in government on a range of affordable-housing priorities at the state level.

The proposal has been in the air at City Hall for months. It was in a draft version of the mayors affordable-housing strategy, but was dropped before it was presented in May, people familiar with the matter said.

With a Republican-led state Senate, elected with the help of generous donations from the real-estate industry, developers are in a strong political position going into the legislative session that opens in January.

Still, the willingness of some developers to back the proposal could help demonstrate they are supporting affordable housing and create goodwill with the administration as the real-estate industry plans to lobby lawmakers seeking the upper-hand on policies such as 421-a. Tenant groups are advocating to push back against developers and landlords on those issues, which come up for renewal in the spring.

It certainly may be that they support this to get what they want on the housing and rent control laws, said Mitchell Moss, a New York University urban planning and policy professor.

Some in the industry questioned the effectiveness of the tax as a revenue stream. Douglas Durst, chairman of the Durst Organization, said he wouldnt oppose raising the mansion tax, but said it was an ineffective way to raise money because it could largely dry up if the market slows down.

And Gary Barnett, the developer of One57, said he was against a proposal that would target the top end of the market. Anything thats discriminatory against a class of owners, visitors, buyers in New York City sends the wrong message and will be counterproductive, he said.

He was more supportive of the potential for a broad-based increase in the mansion tax to assist affordable housing, saying, I dont think thats crazy.

Personal Finance
... Home Appraisers Wont Tell You
By Daniel J. Goldstein
1166 words
15 November 2014
15:14
The Wall Street Journal Sunday
SNJR
WSJSunday
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The impact of the real-estate bubble has only made appraisers harder to work with.

1Were working under a cloud.

There are about 80,000 real-estate appraisers in the U.S., and they play a key role in most home sales: Until they weigh in with a determination of a propertys value, the buyer typically cant finalize a mortgage. (Appraisers also play roles in property-tax appeals and home-equity lending, among other transactions.)

The profession got a reputational black eye, however, during the housing bust, when some appraisers were accused of fudging their numbers so that unscrupulous lenders could approve loans for unqualified buyers.

As a reaction to such allegations, the Dodd-Frank Wall Street Reform and Protection Act of 2010 required more state and federal supervision of appraisers, and put more pressure on lenders to work through appraisal management companies, or AMCs.

These independent firms are designed to keep appraisers and lenders from getting too cozy, explains Sam Heskel, manager of appraisal firm Nadlan Valuation in Brooklyn, N.Y. The law also gave consumers greater recourse to complain to federal regulators about appraisers behavior.

But appraisers say that new rules have made the process slower and more complicated for consumersand that they havent stopped some appraisers from yielding to pressure from banks.

2We dont know your neighborhood.

One unintended consequence of the Dodd-Frank reforms: AMCs are increasingly sending out-of-county or out-of-state appraisers to calculate property values. They may not know the area all that well, and not use the right [comparisons], says Phil Huff, CEO of Platinum Data Solutions, an appraisal-data company in Aliso Viejo, Calif.

This matters because inaccurate appraisals can hurt consumers. An appraisal that undervalues a property may keep a seller from getting a fair price; one thats too high could put a mortgage out of reach for a buyer.

3We work for the bank, not for you...

The typical appraisal costs between $350 and $500, according to Zillow, and its paid for by the consumer (usually the buyer). But the appraisal is actually owned by the bank, and in some cases, homeowners and buyers are adversely affected by appraisals they never personally see.

Consumers do have recourse: Federal law requires that a copy be given to consumers who request it in writing.

4But banks still dont trust us.

During the housing bust, many banks tried to resell homes theyd foreclosed ononly to find that their real value was far lower than the appraiser had suggested. The home wasnt in the condition the appraiser said it was, and sometimes the home wasnt even there, says Greg Schroeder, president of Comergence Compliance Monitoring, a Mission Viejo, Calif., appraisal-technology company.

In part to satisfy the banks, regulations now require appraisers to fill out more paperwork, with the size of the typical appraisal record having doubled to about 30 pages. Most of this work is defensive in nature, says Lora Helt, an appraiser in San Jose. If the deal goes bad, the first person that gets blamed is the appraiser.

5Get a second opinion (maybe in advance).

When an appraisers decision jeopardizes a deal, consumers are likely to feel aggrieved. The Appraisal Institute, an industry trade group, says that such situations arent uncommon, especially in distressed markets, and it encourages consumers to get a second opinion.

But some real-estate pros advocate getting an independent valuation estimate, even before the appraiser shows up. Buyers and sellers can ask their real-estate agents to provide a broker price opinion: Those estimates themselves may not always be accepted by lenders, but they can give the borrower a baseline from which to judge an official appraisals accuracy.

Mr. Heskel advises that buyers get at least two opinions, even if theyre paying cash. Differences in estimates can knock $10,000 to $20,000 off the final sale price of a home, he says.

6Its too hard to get this job.

In many states, it takes relatively little training to qualify as a home inspector or real-estate agent. But for appraisers, simply becoming a trainee requires 75 hours of classroom time, according to the Appraisal Institute. To become a licensed residential appraiser is another 150 hours of classroom time; higher levels of certification have even heavier requirements.

Given that some residential appraisers earn only about $150 per job after the AMCs take their cut, many would-be trainees are put off by the educational hurdles, says Mr. Schroeder: Theres too much work for too little money.

7Our ranks are shrinking (and aging).

In 2010, there were 90,000 appraisers in the U.S. By last June, that figure had shrunk more than 10%, according to the Appraisal Institute, which says its ranks are thinning due to retirements and fewer people entering the profession. Today, more than half of appraisers are between 51 and 65 years old.

8Weve got some bad news about your home makeover.

Some homeowners assume that spending $50,000 on, say, a fancy new kitchen will raise the value of their house by $50,000. But thats seldom the case: Ambitious home-improvement projects are often not worth the price in a resale situation, says Lance Coyle, incoming president of the Appraisal Institute.

Which renovations are more likely to raise a homes value? A fresh coat of paint in neutral colors, or new fixtures, can add value the fastest, says Mr. Coyle, as can green renovations that pay the homeowner back in lower utility bills.

9Dont follow us around.

Some homeowners confuse the roles of home inspectors (who assess the physical condition of a home) and appraisers (who estimate its sale value). One key way in which they differ: While many inspectors want consumers to follow them aroundso they arent shocked when theyre presented with a multi-page fix-it listexperts say theres no benefit for the owner or buyer in tagging along with the appraiser.

10Someday, drones could do our jobs.

A typical appraisal takes about eight hours to complete, even if only an hour is spent at the property itself, says Ms. Helt, the San Jose appraiser. Behind the scenes, appraisers gather property records and data on land values and check features of the home against compssimilar homes sold in the same area.

But technology is speeding up the process. Big Data software has eased the gathering of real-estate information, while tools like Google Earth are figuring in more appraisers research. Even drones could soon come into play, if regulators allow their commercial use. I dont think theres a replacement for boots on the ground, but the precise way a piece of collateral information is collected will change, Mr. Huff says.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Fosun to Expand Further Into Overseas Real Estate
By Esther Fung
883 words
12 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
19
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Corrections & Amplifications

A Chinese insurer agreed to buy the Waldorf Astoria hotel in New York City in October. A Business & Finance article Wednesday about Fosun International Ltd. looking to expand its real-estate holdings incorrectly said the deal happened in November.

(WSJA November 13, 2014)

(END)

SHANGHAI -- Chinese conglomerate Fosun International Ltd. is looking to expand its real-estate holdings abroad, even as foreign competitors bridle at the high prices Chinese buyers have been paying for commercial property, homes and other assets.

Fosun made a splash last year when it purchased New York's Chase Manhattan Plaza for $725 million and London's Lloyds Chambers for GBP 64.5 million ($102 million).

Now, backed by bank loans and funds from insurers, Fosun is on the hunt for real estate in cities such as Los Angeles, Paris, Hamburg, Frankfurt, Lisbon and Sydney, said Xu Xiaoliang, president of Fosun Property, the company's real-estate arm. The firm is finalizing a deal in Los Angeles that could close by the end of the year, he said.

Mr. Xu, in an interview in his Shanghai office overlooking the Huangpu River and downtown skyline, said Fosun is looking for office, hotel and residential assets and is interested in completed buildings and development projects.

The U.S., with its expanding economy and well-established regulatory framework, is an attractive market, Mr. Xu said.

Fosun also is eyeing property-services firms. In May, it bought Tokyo-based real-estate management firm IDERA Capital Management Ltd. for an undisclosed sum.

Since the group delisted its property unit from the Hong Kong Stock Exchange in 2011, Fosun has placed less emphasis on China's hotel and office sectors, given oversupply problems and intensified competition, and is focusing more on niche projects such as senior-living centers.

Fosun Property expects to more than triple its assets under management to 500 billion yuan ($81.7 billion) by 2020 from 140 billion yuan. It also plans to boost the share of its overseas real estate to 50% of its portfolio from 21%.

While overseas expansion could boost Fosun's profits and act as a hedge against a downturn in China, it also carries risks, analysts said. "They've only just started buying property abroad in recent years," said Kai Hu, senior credit officer in Moody's Corporate Finance Group, adding that there could be "refinancing risks if the cash flow from their real-estate operations can't cover their loan repayment at its maturity."

Fosun is one of many Chinese individual and institutional investors buying more real estate abroad to diversify operations in the face of a domestic downturn.

But this has created tension in some countries, including Australia, Singapore and Canada, amid complaints that rivals are getting priced out of the market by flush Chinese buyers.

This month, a Chinese insurer agreed to buy the Waldorf Astoria hotel in New York City from Hilton Worldwide Holdings Inc. for $1.95 billion, the highest price ever for a U.S. hotel, brokers said, although it isn't the highest on a per-room basis.

In Australia, foreign investors bought about one of every six new or yet-to-be-built houses or apartments sold in the third quarter, according to a National Australia Bank survey. That has helped fuel price increases of about 10% in Australian homes nationwide over the past year and nearly 15% in Sydney.

Lawmakers there have come under pressure to respond. Philip Lowe, deputy governor of the Reserve Bank of Australia, reminded banks in a recent speech to be prudent in mortgage lending and be mindful of risks as home prices soar. "At least some parts of the housing market have become unbalanced and this has generated some increase in overall risk," Mr. Lowe said.

Mr. Xu of Fosun said higher prices are a normal result of cities becoming more international, as seen in Australia.

"This to a certain extent has to do with investment demand," he said.

Many of China's recent overseas investments seem less driven by profit than a desire to learn the ropes in overseas markets, property consultant Cushman & Wakefield said in a recent report.

Chinese institutional investment in overseas property totaled $5.1 billion in the first half of this year, up from just $75.2 million in 2008, according to data providers Real Capital Analytics and Cushman & Wakefield.

Property consultants said the Chinese march abroad in some ways resembles Japan's 1980s buying spree of foreign offices, hotels and golf courses. "But this wave of interest from the Chinese is more sustainable," said Anthony Couse, managing director, East Asia at JLL, formerly known as Jones Lang LaSalle, a property consultant. "We see a difference in the motivation."

While Japanese investors leveraged inflated property values at home to acquire offshore assets, Chinese buyers are intent on diversifying risks and globalizing operations, Mr. Couse said.

Chinese property developers also are drawing on their customer base by focusing on foreign cities where Chinese customers want to buy homes.

"We have to link our expansion plans with migration and employment trends," Fosun's Mr. Xu said, adding that the U.S. is a popular destination.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

Deutsche Bank Nears $2 Billion Property Sale; German Bank Seeking to Capitalize on Buoyant U.S. Commercial Real-Estate Market
By Eyk Henning
571 words
12 November 2014
04:05
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

FRANKFURTDeutsche Bank AG is close to selling most of a $2 billion portfolio of commercial real-estate loans to U.S. private-equity firm TPG, people familiar with the matter said.

The deal is driven by an upswing in the U.S. commercial real-estate market, which will allow Deutsche Bank to post a profit on the sale, these people said.

The transaction is subject to regulatory approval but could be signed as soon as this month, these people said. Deutsche Bank, Germanys largest lender, will retain a minority stake in the portfolio.

The assets to be sold include mortgages for shopping malls and office buildings originated by Deutsche Bank and outstanding debt that it bought on the market.

The sale comes amid Deutsche Banks effort to reduce assets on its balance sheet to mitigate effects of increasingly strict banking rules. The German lender aims by the end of next year to shed 250 billion ($313 billion) in assets, weighted by their risk. At the end of September it had already disposed of nearly 200 billion in assets, or roughly 80% of its target.

The U.S. commercial real-estate market has surged recently. Banks issued $28 billion in commercial mortgage-backed securities, or CMBS, in the third quarter, the highest quarterly level since the housing crisis hit in 2007, according to data from Commercial Mortgage Alert. Deutsche Bank is poised to sweep the four major league tables for underwriters and lenders of these securities. The bank said in May it would continue investing in strengthening its U.S. commercial real estate financing business.

Booming demand for commercial real estate securities is increasingly attracting private-equity firms, who are amassing funds from investors looking to bet on rising commercial-property prices around the world. TPG is pushing a step further by snapping up commercial real-estate brokers that link buyers to sellers and landlords to renters.

A venture led by TPG last week completed a $1.05 billion acquisition of Chicago-based DTZ, a real estate services firm with 209 offices in 52 countries and more than $1.9 billion in annual revenue. TPG plans to merge DTZ with Cassidy Turley, a Washington, D.C., firm, which the venture is set to buy before the end of the year, in a deal valued at up to $600 million.

Cassidy Turley is a leading real estate services business in the U.S. and will complement DTZs existing very strong businesses in Asia and Europe as well as DTZs existing U.S. businesses, Ben Gray, Managing Partner Asia at TPG said in September.

The commercial real-estate industry has seen a flurry of acquisitions in recent years, partly in response to globalization. As companies have opened offices throughout the world, they increasingly have preferred real-estate firms that can help them solve their space needs in numerous locations.

Deutsche Banks portfolio being sold is held by its special situation group, which is headed by Jonathan Pollack, the banks global head of commercial real estate and head of risk for structured finance. Mr. Pollack, a leading figure in commercial real estate financing, previously helped start the banks European commercial real-estate business and ran the European commercial mortgage-backed securities trading desk.

Toll Brothers Revenue Rises 29% on West Coast Demand; Average Home Price Increases to $747,000
By Chelsey Dulaney
204 words
10 November 2014
02:45
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Toll Brothers Inc. said revenue in its October quarter surged 29%, lifted by strong demand in the home builders West Coast division.

Toll reported preliminary revenue of $1.35 billion for the quarter ended Oct. 31, topping the $1.31 billion expected by analysts polled by Thomson Reuters.

Revenue in its West Coast division jumped 25%, while revenue in its mid-Atlantic division climbed 17%.

Meanwhile, Tolls average home price increased to $747,000 from $703,000 a year ago, as the company continues to benefit from a slowly improving housing market.

For the first time in four quarters, our contracts were up in both dollars and units compared with the prior years same period, said Chief Executive Douglas C. Yearley, Jr.

The value of Tolls contracts increased 16%, while the value of its backlog edged up 3.4%. A higher backlog in its West Coast division was offset by a decline in its mid-Atlantic division.

Toll Brothers reported the preliminary results ahead of Thursdays investor meetings. The company will report results for the quarter on Dec. 10.

MANSION --- Make Room For the In-Laws --- Want to boost your home value? Invite your mother-in-law over; Why space for aging parents is a hot real-estate amenity now
By Katy McLaughlin
1128 words
7 November 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
M1
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

When a 7-acre property in Silicon Valley went on the market for $14.85 million, a busload of real-estate agents arrived for a tour. The draw? In addition to the resort-style pool and the putting green, the agents, who specialize in finding upscale properties for Asian buyers, wanted to see the two guesthouses -- ideal spaces for aging parents and in-laws.

"I probably did $18 million worth of business with Asians in the last 12 months," said Arthur Sharif, associate broker at Sotheby's International Realty in San Francisco who is listing the estate in Woodside, Calif. "A huge, huge part of the conversation with these folks is the side house," and how it can accommodate elderly relatives, he said.

Put away the snarky in-law jokes. For both domestic and foreign buyers, the hottest amenity in real estate these days is an in-law unit, an apartment carved out of an existing home or a stand-alone dwelling built on the homeowners' property. While the adult children get the peace of mind of having mom and dad nearby, real-estate agents say the in-law accommodations are adding value to their homes.

In an analysis of real-estate listings, homes with in-law units, technically called accessory-dwelling units (ADUs), were priced about 60% higher than houses without them, according to real-estate website Zillow, which examined the past four years of new listings in major cities.

In a 2012 survey of 550 homeowners with one or more living parents by PulteGroup, one of the nation's largest home builders, 32% of respondents said they expected to have an aging relative live with them in the future. In its Southwest markets, Pulte last year rolled out "casitas," stand-alone in-law units; in the Southeast the company has introduced MultiGen dwellings that are built into the main home. Miami-based Lennar, another large builder, introduced NextGen dwellings in late 2011 that are part of the main home but have a separate entrance. Sales of homes with NextGen spaces grew by 27% year-over-year, said Jeff Roos, Lennar's Western region president.

Toll Brothers, a luxury builder based in Horsham, Pa., has been building more communities that include both large, single-family homes and smaller houses designed for empty nesters, said chief marketing officer Kira Sterling.

Architects also cite an uptick in requests for in-law units. "We hardly ever do a house anymore that doesn't have multiple buildings," often as dwellings for aging parents, said Michael Frederick, an architect in Beaufort, S.C.

He and his architect wife, Jane, combined two bedrooms in their home to make a one-bedroom apartment for Ms. Frederick's mother, Ann Lunsford Young. At 81 years old and coping with health issues, Ms. Young downsized from her 3,100-square-foot home in Tennessee and now rents the 500-square-foot apartment in her daughter and son-in-law's home. Ms. Young paid for the renovation, which cost about $40,000 and included transforming a walk-in closet into a kitchenette and installing a skylight.

Likewise, Alyce Pollock, 89, moved from a 2,800-square-foot house in Watsonville, Calif., and now rents a 1,000-square-foot apartment in Santa Cruz, Calif., on the property owned by her daughter, Janet Pollock, and son-in-law, architect Mark Primack. The couple spent $450,000 to build an 1,800-square-foot house on a lot they owned next door to their own home. The house includes the two-story unit where Ms. Pollock lives and a small studio for Mr. Primack.

Ms. Young and Ms. Pollock both say that having their children living just steps away helped ease the transition.

"I had to make new friends and find new exercises, and find a new fit for what I'm doing," Ms. Pollock said. "But it's great. I'm never lonely and I always know they are right next door."

Still, moving meant each woman had to pare down a lifetime of belongings. "I went from 3,100 square feet to 500. But really, this is all I need," said Ms. Young.

Minimalism, however, is in the eye of the beholder. Seeing Ms. Pollock fill the sleek, modern space he designed with her lifelong furniture and "tchotchkes" gave him a jolt, Mr. Primack said. "The architect has to let go," he sighed.

Until recently, homeowners in many areas weren't able to build in-law units on their properties because of local regulations that made them either illegal or too complex to build. But efforts by lobbying groups like AARP and individuals like Portland-based ADU blogger and educator Kol Peterson have led to loosened rules in some areas, advocates say.

Fully permitted in-law units see the highest property values in areas where short-term rentals are both in-demand and legal, brokers in various parts of the country said.

The Frederick/Young home would likely list in the $800,000 range, said broker Edward Dukes, managing partner of Lowcountry Real Estate, which sells luxury property. The Primack/Pollock house might list for around $1 million, said Lauren Spencer, a Coldwell Banker broker in Santa Cruz.

In Oakland, Calif., Larson Shores Architects recently launched a division called Inspired Independence to focus on housing solutions for aging parents.

In August, Rosan Gomperts and her wife, Kim Gaumer, installed a Larson Shores-designed dwelling in the backyard of their Mountain View, Calif., home. Ms. Gomperts' parents, Connie, 92, and Robert, 87, paid about $200,000 for the construction and moved cross-country from Virginia into the prefabricated, wheelchair-accessible home.

While both women are happy to have the elderly couple close by, it has been surprisingly stressful to deal with their many health issues. "It's unclear how sustainable this all is, in all honesty," said Ms. Gomperts.

Inger and Norbert Bischofberger have had the opposite -- albeit less challenging -- experience. In 2004, the couple bought a $5.5 million, 6,700-square-foot house in Hillsborough, partly with the idea that they would build a smaller house for Ms. Bischofberger's father on the property. In 2009, they completed construction on a 405-square-foot Tudor-style in-law cottage. But her 93-year-old father has still not moved in, because he is doing so well and is happy living on his own, Ms. Bischofberger said.

"To disrupt that just for the sake of occupying what I built would be wrong," she said.

License this article from Dow Jones Reprint Service[http://www.djreprints.com/link/DJRFactiva.html?FACTIVA=wjco20141107000002]

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

The Hottest Home Amenity: In-Law Apartments; Want to boost your home value? Invite your mother-in-law over. Why space for aging parents is a hot real-estate amenity now.

Economy
First-Time Home Buyers Fade Further; Chastened by Crash, Initial Entrants Comprise Dwindling Share of Market
By Joe Light
652 words
3 November 2014
12:12
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

First-time home buyers made up the smallest share of U.S. buyers in nearly three decades, a traditionally solid slice of the housing market whose absence is raising questions about the impact of the crash on potential homeowners.

The finding, in a report by the National Association of Realtors, said about 33% of home buyers were first-time purchasers during the 12-month period that ended in June. That is down from 38% in the year-earlier period and off from the 40% that the group said is the historical norm.

Since the financial crisis, first-time buyers have contended with a confluence of factors making a purchase more difficult. Wages and benefits have been relatively stagnant, growing only 0.5% per quarter on average since the recession, according to a Labor Department index released last week.

And the average down payment, typically a hurdle for new buyers, was 18% in September, only slightly lower than the 19% put down in September 2013, according to Ellie Mae, a mortgage software provider that tracks the characteristics of loans run through its platform.

Rising rents and repaying student-loan debt makes saving for a down payment more difficult, especially for young adults whove experienced limited job prospects and flat wage growth since entering the workforce, said NAR chief economist Lawrence Yun.

Beyond the issues of affordability, some renters might be putting off home purchases because of the damage they saw housing do to the last generation of buyers, said Doug Duncan, chief economist of mortgage-finance company Fannie Mae.

Mr. Duncan said that when looking at reasons for not buying, renters typically dont put tight mortgage credit as one of the primary concerns. Today, demand weakness trumps credit tightness, he said.

Jill Moylan, a broker and owner of Home Advantage Realty in Columbia, S.C., said that she thinks many recent college and professional graduates have had less confidence in finding and keeping jobs, leading them to choose to rent rather than buy immediately. She said that as a result, some move-up buyers, who would typically sell their starter home to move to a larger one, are instead keeping the property and renting it out.

Confidence in the economy is really what we need, Ms. Moylan said.

Sam Amadon, 34 years old, bought his first home in Columbia, S.C. in July. Mr. Amadon said that home prices in his area didnt experience the large rise and crash that some other parts of the country did, and that he and his wife are confident they wont lose money on the homethough they also dont expect a windfall.

It seems like theres a possibility of it being a wealth-building tool and that we wouldnt be throwing away so much money on rent. But its really just that we like the house, he said.

Builders have been slow to commit to the first-time buyer segment. Before the housing bust, first-time buyers made up about 40% of the business of Home South Communities LLC, a builder in Suwanee, Ga., said chief financial officer Stephen Palmer. Now, such buyers make up merely 5% of the builders business, which Mr. Palmer said is in part a result of little demand from younger buyers.

Millennials saw their parents take a beating on a house. The initial inclination is, I dont want to get tied down by a house. I saw what happened to my parents, Mr. Palmer said. He said that the profit margins on entry-level homes are also too low to warrant building them in a slow sales environment.

The NAR results were based on responses from 6,572 primary home buyers in July. The NAR has conducted the survey since 1981.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.
