Commercial Property: Manhattan Office Leasing Is Heating Up
By Keiko Morris
738 words
6 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Employers' rising confidence in the economy and their determination to woo skilled workers with hip office environments fueled increased rents and competition for prime Manhattan space during the third quarter, brokers said.

Manhattan's office-vacancy rate at September's end stood at 9.7% -- its lowest level since the third quarter of 2008, according to real estate services firm JLL.

Overall average asking rents also rose, climbing to $64.91 a square foot, up less than 1% from the midyear but a 6.2% increase from the third quarter of 2013.

"Companies generally are doing better so they have capital now and confidence to make moves," said Peter Riguardi, JLL president of the New York tri-state region. "The battle for talent and balance for the work-live lifestyle is so apparent in the decisions for real estate right now."

Increases in asking rents for two of the three major office submarkets were slight, but brokers noted that prices continued on their upward trend.

Midtown South's vacancy rate fell to 6.7% in the third quarter from 7.7% in the previous quarter. The area's average asking rent fell to $58.83 a square foot from $60.09 at midyear, as new deals removed some of the higher-priced offices from the market, according to JLL.

Midtown's vacancy rate declined to 10.1%, and average asking rents grew less than 1% in the second quarter to $70.29. Lower Manhattan's vacancy rate fell to 10.6% from 12.1% in the previous quarter, as average asking rates increased slightly, from $52.85 a square foot to $53.12.

Among the larger new deals signed in the third quarter: law firm Paul Hastings LLP's move to 180,000 square feet in the MetLife Building; and digital advertising firm R/GA Media Group Inc.'s decision to take 173,000 square feet at Brookfield Property Partners LP's Hudson Yards District development.

Retailer Hudson's Bay Co., the parent of Saks Fifth Avenue, signed leases to consolidate its New York offices downtown in about 400,000 square feet at Brookfield Place.

New leases rather than renewals made up much of the deal activity throughout the year, said Donald N. Noland Jr., managing director of tri-state research at real estate services firm Cushman & Wakefield Inc. Average new lease transactions exceeded 2 million square feet for each of the past 13 months, breaking the previous record of 12 consecutive months in 2010 and 2011, according to Cushman.

Renewals tend to happen during soft economic times or periods of recovery. Over the past year large tenants have been on the move -- in part to attract new employees and keep old ones, Mr. Noland said.

Through the third quarter, new leases totaled 24.4 million square feet. The year is on track to surpass the 30.1 million square feet of new leasing in 2011, a once-in-a-decade year, Mr. Noland said.

"This strong growth in occupied space is driven in part by the return of the megadeal," Mr. Noland said.

While much of the leasing was for the highest-quality space, offices considered in the lower-tier category, class B, also have fared well. At 28 W. 44th St. in the Grand Central area, APF Properties has seen rents rise 10% to 15% as a result of both an improving real-estate market and upgrades to the 1920s-era tower.

One tenant in the building was looking for spaces in Midtown South but reversed course and stayed because of escalating rents in that market, said Berndt Perl, principal of APF Properties.

Midtown South class B rents are higher than Midtown, Mr. Perl said. So Midtown is a pretty good deal compared to Midtown South.

The downtown rental market has bolstered sales of office property, with many buyers opting to retain them as commercial space. Sales of office buildings 200,000 square feet and larger hit 9.3 million square feet, worth about $3.5 billion in the third quarter, according to Newmark Grubb Knight Frank. The three-year annual average is 5.8 million square feet.

"This is now a destination rather than an alternative that was purely price driven," said Hal Stein, executive vice president and managing director at Newmark Grubb Knight Frank.

A Tale of Two Taxes --- Proposed for New York, Mansion Surcharge Failed to Cool London Real Estate
By Josh Barbanel
935 words
4 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A fear of annual mansion taxes on the wealthy, many of them foreigners, is causing ripples in luxury housing markets on both sides of the Atlantic.

In London, an Italian hedge-fund executive withdrew an offer a few weeks ago for a 4,000-square-foot home in the prime Knightsbridge neighborhood listed for about GBP 20 million ($32 million).

He was worried, said his broker, Charles McDowell, about a proposal floated by the opposition Labour Party to raise nearly GBP 1.2 billion ($2 billion) through a new annual tax on the value of high-price homes.

In New York City, broker Caroline Guthrie of Brown Harris Stevens said she saw a sale fall apart between her Brazilian buyers and the owner of a $30 million Park Avenue co-op.

The breakdown, Ms. Guthrie said, followed remarks by Mayor Bill de Blasio that he was evaluating a plan to impose an annual property-tax surcharge on expensive pied-a-terres.

"The buyers have been advised by their financial people that it is not wise to proceed while this in on the table," she said.

Ms. Guthrie said other potential buyers raised similar worries spurred by their lawyers, accountants and advisers.

But brokers and other lawyers say the uncertainty in the New York and London markets may be worse than the actual bite of whatever new taxes are adopted -- if any.

A few years ago, when a wave of new less onerous taxes hit the luxury international home market in London, sales stalled briefly but rebounded and now are as strong as ever.

Prices rose after these taxes were imposed but more modestly than before. Prices on expensive homes across England and Wales rose also more slowly than lower-priced homes not facing the higher taxes.

In recent decades, and especially since the financial crisis of 2008, luxury homes in both New York and London have been seen by international buyers as a haven for their assets.

Apartment prices have set new records in New York and prices for London properties near the top of the market soared to 31.8% above the 2008 peak, according to data from Knight Frank LLP, a global real-estate company.

But the flow of wealth has changed neighborhoods and has made the wealthy more of a target for tax collectors.

"It is the same trend," said Liam Bailey, global head of residential research at a Knight Frank. "London and New York are being more internationalized. Locals get pushed out as the market becomes more unaffordable."

In 2011, the United Kingdom increased a one-time real estate transfer tax to 5% on properties valued at more than GBP 1 million ($1.6 million).

The following year, the top rate of the levy, known as the stamp duty land tax, was set at 7% for properties valued at more than GBP 2 million ($3.2 million), with a rate of 15% for many high-price residential properties owned by offshore and other corporate entities, in an effort to cut down on tax avoidance and evasion.

Capital-gains taxes also were approved for both corporations and foreign homeowners who don't pay local taxes. They pay a similar federal tax in the U.S.

The increases were seen as a "massive attack on mostly foreigners or as we call them non-domiciliaries," said Charles Hutton, a partner at Speechly Bircham, a London law firm who works with wealthy individuals.

The new tax regime also sowed confusion and worry about the impact on the market. Buyers rushed to close before changes took effect.

Others stopped using corporations to buy houses. But it didn't turn out to be the disaster that he said everyone expected.

"The last couple of years the London market has continued to roar away," he said. "A lot of people just regard the London market as such a good investment that even with a bit of tax on it is still pretty good."

The New York tax, under a bill introduced Monday in Albany, would impose an annual tax on a sliding scale beginning at 0.5% for the value of properties over $5 million, up to 4% for more than $25 million.

James Parrott, deputy director and chief economist at the Fiscal Policy Institute, a left-leaning research group that proposed the new tax, said the middle class was being priced out in both New York and London.

He said that even if the new taxes had some "restraining effect on prices on the high end" in the United Kingdom, "it doesn't seem like it has destroyed the market."

But brokers and developers in both London and New York say they worry that proposals for annual taxes could have more impact on buyer behavior than one time transfer tax like the stamp duty tax in the U.K.

Transfer taxes by New York City and New York state are 2.8% for properties that sell for $1 million or more.

James Gilbert-Green, a broker and partner at Strutt & Parker in London, said that prices in London already have stalled "as a direct result of a lack of certainty in the market on the future taxes."

Even multimillionaires and billionaires with multiple homes who are willing to pay large sums for another home, don't like to be told they will have to pay extra taxes every year, Ms. Guthrie said.

"It is not appealing," she said.

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

U.S. News: Housing, Consumer Numbers Down
By Kathleen Madigan
441 words
1 October 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

U.S. consumers unexpectedly pulled back on their economic optimism in September, while the yearly growth in home prices nationwide slowed more than anticipated in midsummer, according to two reports released Tuesday.

The Conference Board, a private research group, said its index of consumer confidence dipped to 86.0 in September from a revised 93.4 in August, first reported as 92.4.

The August index was the highest reading since October 2007, before the last recession started. The September drop puts confidence back to its lowest reading since May. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had forecast the latest index to edge up to 92.8.

The present situation index, a gauge of consumers' assessment of current economic conditions, dropped to 89.4 this month from a revised 93.9 in August, originally put at 94.6.

The Conference Board pointed to worries about the labor markets.

"A less positive assessment of the current job market, most likely due to the recent softening in growth, was the sole reason for the decline in consumers' assessment of present-day conditions," said Lynn Franco, director of economic indicators at the Conference Board.

On the housing front, the home-price index covering the U.S. increased 5.6% in the 12 months that ended in July, according to the S&P/Case-Shiller Home Price Index report.

That is down from 6.3% in June. Nationwide, home prices were rising at double-digit yearly rates as recently as February 2014.

The home-price index covering 10 major U.S. cities increased 6.7% in the year that ended in July. The 20-city price index also was up 6.7%, less than the 7.3% expected by economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal.

On an unadjusted basis, the national index increased 0.5% in July over June, while the 10-city index and the 20-city composite each increased 0.6%. Seasonally adjusted, the U.S. index increased 0.2% in July, the 10-city gauge and the 20-city composite each fell 0.5%.

"While the year-over-year figures are trending downward, home prices are still rising month-to-month although at a slower rate than what we are used to seeing over the past couple of years," said David M. Blitzer, chairman of the Index Committee at S&PDow Jones Indices. "The rise in August new home sales -- which are not covered by the S&P/Case-Shiller indices -- is a welcome exception to recent trends."

US
U.S. Home Price Growth Slows Again in July, Says S&P/Case-Shiller; Slower Pace of Home-Price Appreciation Consistent With Other Housing Data
By Kathleen Madigan
346 words
30 September 2014
15:32
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
WSJ.com
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The yearly growth in home prices across the U.S. slowed more than expected in the middle of summer, according to a home price report released Tuesday.

The home-price index covering the entire nation increased 5.6% in the 12 months ended in July, said the S&P/Case-Shiller Home Price Index report. That is down from 6.3% in June. U.S. home prices were rising at double-digit yearly rates as recently as February 2014.

The home-price index covering 10 major U.S. cities increased just 6.7% in the year ended in July. The 20-city price index was also up 6.7%, less than the 7.3% expected by economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal.

On an unadjusted basis, the national index increased 0.5% in July over June, while the 10-city index and the 20-city composite each increased 0.6%. Seasonally adjusted, the U.S. index increased 0.2% in July, the 10-city gauge and the 20-city composite each fell 0.5%.

"While the year-over-year figures are trending downward, home prices are still rising month-to-month although at a slower rate than what we are used to seeing over the past couple of years," said David M. Blitzer, Chairman of the Index Committee at S&PDow Jones Indices.

"The slower pace of home price appreciation is consistent with most of the other housing data on housing starts and home sales," he said. "The rise in August new home sales--which are not covered by the S&P/Case-Shiller indices--is a welcome exception to recent trends."

Regionally, cities in the south and west that saw the largest price cuts during the recession, are seeing some of the strongest price increases now. But even in areas like Las Vegas and Miami, the yearly gains have slowed.
